χ2 Q∗(·| x) Q(·| x) + χ2 QY(·| x) Q (·| x) + 1 

i

(3.28) 40 To appreciate this error, consider for instance that one way for the inference sampler Q(·| x) to be suboptimal is to miss the modes of the posterior Q∗(·| x). Then, most of the samples Z(i) ∼ Q(·| x)tend to have small likelihood P ≤| Z(i)|(Z(i)) and thus are not “reasonable interpretations” of x. It follows that the denominator of vn is underestimated, one becomes overly surprised by x, and the amount of update ∥vn∥ is excessive. Similarly, if Q (·| x) misses the modes of QY(·| x), then one misses the valuable ideas in the passage and learns little, and the norm of the numerator of vn is underestimated. Worse still, if both occur, then vn would be a moderately long vector that points in some wrong direction (e.g. towards simply memorizing x and giving up any logical effort, or relying too much on one’s familiar proof routines) and quickly drives one to misunderstanding. Hence, the suboptimality of Q(·| x) and Q (·| x) demands one to sample more to avoid any possible failure, and makes learning inefficient. The error estimate (3.28) captures both sources of sampling error and thus characterizes one’s learning efficiency. Examples 3.1 and 3.2 indicate that the performance and efficiency of both inference and training are determined by the quality of the samplers and the amount of sampling. Hence, in the setting of the representation Proj # Pf , we informally define slow thinking as follows. 

Definition 3.4 (Slow thinking) . Define the target V∗(p, x, θ ) as any operator that takes the following function p as input 

∀x ∈ Σ∗, p(x|θ) := (Proj # Pf )≤| x|(x), f = fθ

A slow thinking algorithm is a consistent estimator of V∗(p, x, θ ), within which any summation over Proj −1 

> <ω

(x) is approximated by sampling. Specifically, given a fixed Proj, denote by Vn(x, θ ) the stochas-tic output of a slow thinking algorithm, given total sampling size n. Then, 

∀θ, ∀x ∈ Σ∗, lim  

> n→∞

error  V∗(p, x, θ ), V n(x, θ ) = 0 almost surely where error denotes any difference function. The slow thinking scaling law of this algorithm is the function 

n 7 → Eerror  V∗(p, x, θ ), V n(x, θ ) 

where expectation is over the randomness of Vn due to sampling. Alternatively, the scaling law may use other input variables, such as the amount of computations (FLOPs) or the actual time of implementation. In contrast, a fast thinking algorithm directly models a distribution in the observable data space instead of using any latent space, e.g. the distributions Pplain .Results from the previous sections can be expressed in terms of Definition 3.4 and are summarized in Table 3.1. It turns out that all scaling laws are proportional to terms of the form χ2/n , and thus all errors go to zero as the sampling size n → ∞ . One can extend the sample x in Definition 3.4 to a pair ( xp, x a) to allow for conditional encoding or to a data distribution P∗.

## 3.6 Prediction vs. Explanation 

This section studies the non-causality of the posterior distribution Q∗ (as well as QY) and its impli-cation to the samplers. A hierarchy of sampler classes is obtained, which leverage increasingly more information from the input data and eventually can reach Q∗ and QY.To begin with, let us consider the simplest sampler. Ideally, one would like to directly decode a latent sequence z from the latent distribution P , without using any separate model or prompt (even a separate prompt would require a separate encoding operation and thus greater cost). Yet, the challenge is to ensure that the sampled z corresponds to the provided data x ∈ Σ∗, namely z ∈ Proj −1 

> <ω

(x). In general this is not guaranteed, but we can consider a special case, when Proj −1 

> <ω

has an easy-to-handle structure. 

Definition 3.5. We say that a projection Proj is “disentangled” if its next-segment map Φ x(z, a )depends only on the observable context x. > <ω

(x) and a ∈ Σ. As a result, the set of latent sequences Proj −1 

> <ω

Definition 3.6. Given a disentangled projection, we call the sampler Qid the identity sampler .The name identity emphasizes that Qid does not involve any model other than P or any initial prompt other than the empty string ∅, i.e. at t = 1 in (3.30), the condition is empty. Note that the identity sampler is “strictly causal” with respect to its input x: Consider the random sequence SS1 . . . S |x| = S, (S, x ) ≃ Z, Z ∼ Qid (·| x), St ∈ S (x<t ) (3.31) By (3.30), the random variable S≤t does not depend on the “present and future” information x≥t. We generalize the identity sampler to capture this causality. 42 Definition 3.7. Given a disentangled projection, a sampler Q ∈ Q Proj is called a predictive sampler 

if for any x ∈ Σ∗ and t = 1 , . . . |x|, the random latent sequence S≤t defined by (3.31) and Q does not depend on x≥t. Denoted the set of predictive samplers by Q

> Proj

.To simplify the math and make our arguments more general, we study a larger class of causal samplers that may depend on the “present” information xt. This is fair, since if we can show that this larger class is inadequate for some goal, then the predictive samplers cannot achieve this goal either. Recall from Lemma 2.1 that any latent sequence z ∈ Proj −1 

> <ω

> <ω

(x). The following definition does not assume that Proj is disentangled.

Since the inquisitive sampler QY (3.22) has similar form as the posterior Q∗, one can expect that the optimal sampler for training is “almost surely” non-causal as well. Since Table 3.1 indicates that the slow thinking scaling laws all depend on χ2(Q∗(·| x)∥Q(·| x)), one may conclude that causal samplers (and thus predictive samplers) are inadequate for slow thinking in terms of sample efficiency. 

Definition 3.9. We will often refer to QProj as explanatory samplers , to emphasize that the causal samplers QÙ 

> Proj

In general, each containment is proper, and in particular, Q∗, Q Y ∈ Q Proj \Q Ù

> Proj

.Besides the rigorous proofs, the difference between predictive and explanatory samplers can be illustrated by intuitive examples. Example 1.1 implies that only explanatory samplers have the suffi-cient information for approximating the posterior sampler Q∗. An analog of Example 1.1 in the text modality will be provided in Figure 5.1. Meanwhile, below is a simpler example for texts. 

Example 3.3. Consider the following two texts, which are identical from the perspective of a human reader. 

x1 =“ . . . by Tychonoff’s theorem, Ω ω is compact . . . ”

x2 =“ . . . Ωω is compact, by Tychonoff’s theorem . . . ”Suppose the projection of Example 2.3 is adopted, so the latent sequences Proj −1 

> <ω

(x) of each text x is x

inserted with thoughts ⟨s⟩y⟨/s ⟩, y ∈ Σ∗. Suppose the latent distribution P is an autoregressive model (2.13), so the latent sequences z ∈ Proj −1 

> <ω

(x) with high probability P ≤| z|(z) are the ones that can be easily understood while being read uni-directionally. In other words, these z are the modes of Q∗(·| x). Since the logic of the text x1 is presented linearly, the latent sequence z = x1 is uni-directionally understandable, so it does not matter whichever sampler we use. However, for x2, a latent sequence of the following form might be necessary 

z = “ . . . ⟨s⟩by Tychonoff’s theorem ⟨/s ⟩ Ωω is compact, by Tychonoff’s theorem . . . ”For explanatory samplers, generating the thought in z is easy, as they can see the entire text x2 while doing this. For predictive samplers, however, what is visible to them is only the preceding context “. . . ”, so they have to come up with the logic on their own, which may have a much smaller chance. As a result, the explanatory samplers are generally more efficient than the handicapped predictive samplers, and this advantage can manifest in all aspects, from better understanding the users’ prompts to extracting more knowledge from training data. Still, one interesting subtlety is that humans solve Example 3.3 in a slightly different manner. When reading x2, we can first attend to “by Tychonoff’s theorem” and then turn to “Ω ω is compact”. So the human agency makes our perception more flexible than autoregressive models. The modeling of this agency will be discussed in Section 6. 

Remark 3.11. The names “predictive/explanatory samplers” come from the notions of “forward predictive inference” and “backward explanatory inference” in cognitive psychology [155, 181] and “inference to the best explanation” in philosophy [103, 37]. A human reader may perform forward prediction to infer the possible subsequent sentences of the currently read sentence, or backward explanation to try to understand a sentence based on the entire article as context. One related notion is “deductive vs. abductive reasoning” [84, 20], such that abduction is the logical derivation of the probable causes of some observations. We do not use these names since the sampling Z ∼ Q(·| x), as a general stochastic procedure, is not limited to the special case of logical inference. 44 The non-causal behavior of the posterior Q∗ might be of independent interest, so we give it a name below. 

Definition 3.10 (Posterior drift) . By Theorem 3.1, given a latent distribution P ∈ P (Ω ω ), an ob-servation sequence x ∈ Σ∗ and an earlier time 1 ≤ t < |x|, the marginal of the posterior distribution 

Q∗(·| x) over the earlier latent sequences Proj −1 

> <ω

(x≤t) is generally different from the posterior Q∗(·| x≤t)given only the “incomplete information” x≤t.

Example 3.4. This posterior drift can be highly non-causal in the sense that for any t0 and arbitrarily large t2 > t 1 ≫ t0, there exist P and x ∈ Σ≥t2 such that ΠProj −1  

> <ω (x≤t0)

#Q∗(·| x≤t1 )̸ = Π Proj −1  

> <ω (x≤t0)

#Q∗(·| x≤t2 )As an informal example, if a novel ends with an incredible twist, readers may reasonably suspect that the author has already planned for this in the beginning. Then, one can set t0, t 1, t 2 to be the opening, the paragraph before the twist, and the ending. So the posterior distribution of the author’s early intention at t0 is updated by a very late observation between t1 and t2. This intuition is in accordance with Example 1.2 and Panel 4 of Example 1.1. (A formal example can be constructed based on the projection of Remark 2.8.) 

Remark 3.12. There may be a misunderstanding that the explanatory samplers are not applicable to inference, as these non-causal samplers seem to require the follow-up text that has not been decoded yet. This is not the case as demonstrated by the decoding methods in Section 3.3. The sampler Q is only involved in the “prefill stage” when the prompt x is encoded, while the decoding stage simply uses the latent model Pf to decode a latent sequence Z(t). Thereby, the scaling law for decoding (Proposition 3.1) only involves χ2(Q∗(·| x)∥Q(·| x)), the divergence over x, and non-causality takes place only within 

x. Intuitively, we are modeling a reader who first reads the text x thoroughly (possibly in a nonlinear order), and then starts writing uni-directionally, as in Example 1.2. 

## 3.7 Summary 

This section has demonstrated that the basic operations of the model Proj # Pf , namely encoding, decoding and training, can be and perhaps must be implemented through the sampling of the latent sequences Proj −1 

> <ω

(x). Thus, a slow thinking model is determined by the tuple (Σ , Ω, Proj , P, Q ), namely the observable vocabulary, the latent vocabulary, the projection Proj : Ω ω ⇀ Σω , the latent distribution, and the (inference) sampler. Optionally, one can include the train sampler Q , and also specify the parametrization of P and the samplers. Then, encoding can be performed by (3.2), conditional encoding by (3.7) and Remark 3.5, decoding by either (3.13) or (3.14), training by either (3.23, 3.25, 3.26) in the two-sampler setting or (3.17, 3.25) in the one-sampler setting. The efficiency of these operations is determined by the alignment between the implemented samplers and the optimal samplers, and this is characterized by a family of slow thinking scaling laws (Table 3.1). The optimal training sampler is distinct from the optimal inference sampler, which mirrors the exploration vs. exploitation problem. These optimal samplers are generally non-causal, so current slow thinking models, which rely on predictive samplers, are suboptimal in terms of sampling variance, and can be improved by using explanatory samplers for both training and prefill. The hierarchy of the samplers, in particular the gap between current practice and optimality, is illustrated in Figure 3.1. 

# 4 Unified Objective 

So far we have constructed the static theory for slow thinking based on two objectives, the optimization of both approximation ability and sampling efficiency. It is conceptually possible, however, to derive the entire static theory from one objective, the maximization of the rate of uncertainty reduction in real time. This objective will also pave the way to the general theory of active lifting. Different from the other sections, this section mainly uses informal arguments. Let P∗ ∈ P (Σ ∗) be any target distribution; for convenience, assume that it has finite entropy and prefix-free support. Let Pθ be an abstract model, parametrized by an abstract parameter θ.Assume that Pθ ranges in some abstract subspace of P(Σ ω ) that includes the plain parametrization 45 Pf , the projection parametrization Proj # Pf for various simple projections, and potentially many other possible parametrizations. As usual, the training objective is cross entropy

The two objectives of the static theory can be derived from this abstract objective. A sketch of argument is provided by Figure 4.1. First of all, the abstract model Pθ should prefer parametrizations with greater approximation ability, e.g. the model Proj # Pf from Psimple instead of Pf from Pplain 

(these subspaces are defined in Section 2.9). Since the former is a strictly larger subspace according to the representation hierarchy, its terminal loss is more “likely” to be zero

where informally the target P∗ is some random variable in P(Σ ∗). Thus, the objective (4.2) is less “likely” to be ∞ when using the projection parametrization. Even if the terminal losses are zero for both Psimple and Pplain , the estimate (2.2) indicates that the training error is generally proportional to the norm of P∗ with respect to the parametrization of the model. As our separation theorems have justified the norm comparison (2.17), it implies that Psimple may enjoy much smaller finite-time losses than Pplain , for large steps s asymptotically. Secondly, whenever possible, the training process should adopt approximate computations that save much time with little deterioration in performance. In our setting, this means reducing the elapsed time ts while maintaining the uncertainty reduction C(θs−1) − C(θs). Latent sampling helps to reduce each ts+1 − ts from something superpolynomial in the input length |x| to the practical 

O(n|x|2) with sampling size n (The computation complexity of Transformers is O(|x|2). Assume that the projection is chosen such that sup x∈Σ≥1 max z∈Proj −1  

> <ω (x)

|z|/|x| < ∞, e.g. for Example 2.3 this means that the constants ct are bounded). Meanwhile, approximating the optimal samplers ( Q → Q∗

and Q → QY) helps to reduce the estimation error of the training gradient, and thus alleviate the loss in the gain C(θs−1) − C(θs). Thirdly, the loss (4.2) may help to determine many details of the implementation of Proj # Pf .Choices such as the following are difficult to decide because they simultaneously increase (or decrease) both accuracy and time cost: 

• The amount of sampling n over the latent sequences Proj −1 

> <ω

(x), for the training of both the latent (3.23) and the sampler(s) (3.25). 

• For projections similar to Example 2.3, how frequently should the sampler insert non-empty thoughts y(t), and how long should each thought be. This can be interpreted as the balancing of fast and slow thinking. 

• Whether to use two samplers Q, Q (3.23) or just one (3.17). 

• Whether the two samplers share the same model, and whether the sampler and the latent dis-tribution share the same model, and so on. If we manage to optimize these variables in an end-to-end fashion, then the optimizers of (4.2) may reproduce many human traits. For instance, the optimized sampler can automatically and flexibly switch to fast thinking (not inserting thoughts y(t)) when reading easy texts, and slow thinking other-wise (producing long thoughts, conditioned on a large portion of the text x), and think for more trials (larger n) when dealing with difficult or multifaceted problems. A more concrete example of using the objective (4.2) to balance fast and slow thinking will be provided in Section 5.6.3. 

# 5 Derivation and Improvement of Existing Models 

To show that our theory encompasses the slow thinking models used in practice, this section derives the design, training and inference of DeepSeek-R1 [69] in a first-principles manner, up to implementation-level specifics. We also discuss possible ways to improve the model, including the optimal samplers and a more expressive form of slow thinking suitable for pretraining. Basically, a slow thinking model is determined by three factors: Its projection Proj (or equivalently, lifting) and its positions in the representation hierarchy (Figure 3.1) and sampler hierarchy (Figure 2.1). The combination for DeepSeek-R1 consists of a Proj that resembles Example 2.3, the “forgetful latent” that will be defined, and the identity sampler (Definition 3.6). This derivation from our general theory is a bit lengthy compared to a direct derivation in the particular setting of DeepSeek-R1. However, such detour makes it easier to improve the model by climbing the sampler hierarchy and representation hierarchy. There will be three stages of improvements, ranked by the amount of modifications: ascending the sampler hierarchy, then the representation hierarchy, and transcending the static theory. 47 5.1 Derivation of Representation

## 5.4 Derivation of Inference 

The inference of DeepSeek-R1 can be derived from the first decoding algorithm of Section 3.3. Suppose as before that the conversation context is x = ( x(t) 

> q

x(t)

)Tt=1 x(T +1)

> /

> r

∼ (Proj # Pf )( ·| x → Σ∗

> □

> □

> <ω

(x). By forgetfulness (Definition 5.1), the next-segment distribution Pf (·| Z → T Σ∗

> □

Xr ∼ Pf (·| xY → Σ∗), Y ∼ Pf (·| x → T ) (5.19) and no sampler is needed. Hence, we have derived the training and inference of DeepSeek-R1, with encoding given by (5.13), decoding given by (5.19), and training by either (5.14, 5.15) with a prescribed target or (5.17, 5.18) with relaxed targets. 

## 5.5 Stage One: Improving Sampling Efficiency 

Next, we move on to the interesting topic of improving the model. This section introduces the first stage that concerns the sampler hierarchy. These easy improvements maintain the existing representation of forgetful latent, so they apply only to the training process. Before going into the technicalities, let us consider a motivating example. 

Example 5.1. Suppose a slow thinking model is finetuned on a pair of user query and high-quality response ( xq , x r ). As depicted in Figure 5.1 (left), it is about computing the constant π in Python. Training proceeds by 53 Query: "Please write a simple Python code that         

> computes π"
> Response: "Excellent question! Computing πis
> a classic programming exercise that beautifully
> blends mathematics, algorithm design, and
> numerical analysis. The Archimedes Method
> (Polygon Approximation) is historically the first
> true algorithm for π. Here is a simple Python
> code that implements this method: ......
> Query Response
> The Leibniz Series: π/4 = 1 - 1/3 + 1/5 ...
> Monte-Carlo: throwing darts at a square
> The Gauss-Legendre Algorithm
> The Archimedes Method with polygons
> Bailey–Borwein–Plouffe (BBP) Formula
> ......

# ☹

# ☹

# ☹

# ☹

# ☹Figure 5.1: Left: A pair of query xq and response xr for finetuning slow thinking models. The response is based on the Archimedes method for computing π. Right: The blue boxes represent the possible thoughts that can be generated by the sampler. Here we assume that a predictive sampler is used, which can only see the query when producing thoughts, and thus the sampled methods for computing π

are rather uniformly distributed over the popular methods. Only one thought guessed the Archimedes method, and the notation ⌢ indicates that the other thoughts are unhelpful or misleading for producing 

xr . The shades of the arrows represent the likelihoods Pf (y|xq → T ) and Pf (xr |xq y → Σ∗

> □

). The former are positive and approximately uniform, while the latter are all vanishingly small except for the right thought. If instead a well-trained explanatory sampler is used, then most of the sampled thoughts would focus on the Archimedes method. 1. Sampling multiple thoughts from the sampler. 2. Encouraging the latent model to generate the response xr conditioned on xq and each thought. 3. Guiding the sampler to produce thoughts that are helpful for step 2. (Recall that the formal description of this training process is provided by (3.23) and (3.25) for the general case, and by (5.14) and (5.15) for the special case with the identity sampler.) The training efficiency depends heavily on the usefulness of the thoughts for deriving xr , and thus on the efficiency of the sampler at producing relevant thoughts. Namely, if the sampler quickly produces a reasonable thought that can effectively lead to xr , then in step 2 the latent model gets an easy job and in step 3 the sampler has a clear target to fit. However, things could fall apart if the query is open-ended with multiple possible answers. As shown in Figure 5.1 (right), there are many simple methods to compute 

π, and it is very unlikely that a randomly chosen method coincides with that of the provided response 

xr . Thus, if a thought is generated without the knowledge of xr , it is probably irrelevant to xr and cannot help with training. This is the case for all predictive samplers, including the identity sampler 

Qid (·| xq ), which can only see xq . The causal samplers QÙ 

> Proj

are no better; by (3.33), they can see the first token of xq , which in this case is “Excellent” and is not really informative. Hence, in this case, it appears that only the explanatory samplers, which can attend to the full response xr , are capable of building a bridge between xq and xr .Example 5.1 (and similarly Examples 1.1 and 1.2) indicate that explanatory samplers are indis-pensable for the proper encoding of sequences. Since Section 5.4 indicates that (the prefill stage of) the inference of DeepSeek-R1 does not involve latent sampling, we only need to consider the application of explanatory samplers to training. Same as the set-up of Section 5.3, suppose the training sample consists of a query x and response 

xr . To implement an explanatory sampler, one can use any prompt that contains both x and xr , for instance the one in Figure 5.2. Optionally, one can instantiate two samplers, the inference sampler Q

and train sampler Q ; one simple way is to add a tag in the prompt that indicates “inference mode” or “train mode” as in the figure. To save storage, the sampler(s) can share the same underlying model (DeepSeek-V3) with the latent distribution Pf ; namely, the underlying model plays Pf if the prompt is simply x, and plays the (inference or train) sampler if the prompt is the following (with the inference or train tag). 54 <Query> 

Please write a simple Python code that computes pi.<eos> 

<Response> 

Here is a Python code based on the Archimedes method ...<eos> 

<Explain> Inference /Train mode

L(n)1 (θ) = − 1

∼ Q(·| xx r ) (5.25) It resembles (5.20) except that the averaging takes place outside of log. The distinction is that effectively (5.20) trains the n-sample estimator pn for inference, whereas (5.25) only trains the 1-sample estimator p1.

Remark 5.1. One might worry if the explanatory sampler Q(·| xx r ) could help the latent model Pf to cheat during training and become degenerate. Specifically, it may appear that Q(·| xx r ) could simply copy-and-paste the answer xr to the sampled thought Y , and then Pf can learn to copy Y to produce 

xr , thus getting a perfect score Pf (xr |xY → Σ∗

> □

) ≈ 1 while learning nothing. This is not the case. Note that the reward in the sampler loss Lsampler is proportional to log Pf (Y | x → T ) + log Pf (xr | xY → Σ∗

> □

)So a good thought Y is like a bridge, connecting the prompt x on one side (maximizing the first term) and the response xr on the other side (maximizing the second term). Cheating (setting Y = xr ) is unhelpful for the first term, and will be avoided by the sampler. If x is a math problem, for instance, and if Pf is an autoregressive model, then Y would be a unidirectional derivation that proceeds step-by-step to the final answer xr . Similarly, since the losses Ln are proportional to these two terms, the latent model Pf is trained to adapt to these bridges. Besides, there is an another way to see that the explanatory samplers are appropriate; no matter what sampler is used, the Monte-Carlo estimator pn

always approximates the same probability value (5.7), so in principle the sampler can use any model and condition on any information. A further improvement could be using a larger sampling size for the training of the latent model, namely setting n > 1 in the loss Ln (5.20) or (5.21). Despite that (5.20) and (5.25) use almost the same compute, the latter has a potential disadvantage, that Pf would try to fit each Y (i)xr regardless of the quality of Y (i) and thus could receive harmful training signal. This problem could be severe if 

x is a difficult problem or if xr covers multiple keypoints, so that the sampled thoughts have a much higher probability to be irrelevant, incorrect or incomplete. In comparison, inside the summation of (5.20), the low-quality thoughts Y (i) correspond to smaller summands and thus are ignored during the forward pass and backpropagation. With a larger n, the hit rate (the probability that at least one of the thoughts {Y (i)}ni=1 is a good thought) approaches 1, making it more likely for Pf to receive a meaningful training signal. Still, as discussed in Remark 3.1, even if n = 1, it is possible for a sampler to implicitly produce multiple thoughts and thus increase the hit rate. However, this strategy might not always work, as it could make the thoughts overly lengthy and it has been observed empirically that the performance of DeepSeek-R1 is negatively correlated with its thought length when above some threshold [109]. As a final remark, all the above improvements are only for the training process. Inference does not need to be modified, since the latent model is forgetful and thus decoding does not involve any sampler. Nevertheless, suppose one is not interested in the sampling of (Proj # Pf )( ·| x → Σ∗

> □

) but wants the maximum likelihood response 

x∗ 

> r

= arg max  

> xr∈Σ∗
> □

(Proj # Pf )( xr |x → Σ∗

> □

)Then, the decoding method (5.19) can be modified to best-of-K sampling for some large KX∗

This section introduces the second stage of improvements, those that require a modified representation. Abstractly speaking, the motivation is to climb the representation hierarchy (Figure 2.1) and make the model more expressive. Intuitively speaking, we want the model to learn reasoning more effectively during pretraining, and to perform more sophisticated reasoning during inference. We advocate for the following two modifications, which need to be applied together to be effective. 1. Ubiquitous thinking: The thoughts y(t) can be inserted anywhere in the text, not just between each pair of query and response in a conversation setting. Using the terminology of Section 5.1, the latent distribution Pf can be supported on the entire Dom(Proj), not just Ω ω

> /

. A natural training objective is needed to encourage the sampler to insert thoughts in an efficient manner, assigning long thoughts only to difficult texts that require deep reasoning. 2. Persistent thinking: Each thought y(t) may have lasting effect on the processing of later texts, e.g. an epiphany we had when reading chapter 5 of “Differential Geometry” may become helpful for understanding chapter 9. In terms of Definition 5.1 and equation (5.5), the latent distribution 

Pf is no longer forgetful, and the likelihood of each segment y(T +1) x(T +1) may depend on the previous thoughts ( y(t))Tt=1 .We first discuss the representation of persistent and ubiquitous slowing thinking, then formulate its explanatory sampler, and derive a training objective that provides an intrinsic motivation to balance fast and slow thinking. One may compare the inserted thoughts ( y(t))Tt=1 to the “working memory” of a human who is reading the text x, since both are persistent, continuously evolving internal states. 

5.6.1 Persistent and Ubiquitous Thinking 

The motivation for “ubiquitous thinking” is to improve the reading ability of LLMs, specifically, enable them to perform deep and efficient slow thinking when encoding any text worthy of reasoning. One beneficial outcome is that we can adapt slow thinking to pretraining to improve data efficiency. Ideally, an LLM should develop most of its reasoning ability in an unsupervised manner during pretraining, while post-training is only for adapting the LLM to specific tasks. For the state-of-the-art LLMs, a considerable portion of their pretrain datasets consists of texts that are rich in reasoning, such as textbooks, research papers and codes [165, 67, 177]. So LLMs should perform slow thinking to squeeze all the reasoning out of these texts, learn it, and master all kinds of reasoning by the end of pretraining. Another benefit is that LLMs can perform slow thinking in the prefill stage of inference to thoroughly understand the context. The motivation for “persistent thinking” is to overcome the limitation of forgetfulness (Definition 5.1) and enhance the approximation ability of the model. As as illustration of this limitation, consider the “cups and ball” game of Figure 2.2. Instead of watching the magician swapping the cups, suppose we are only presented with a written list of the swappings performed. Conceptually, it is impossible to always answer correctly within a bounded amount of time if the number of swappings is unbounded, following the discussion in Section 2.5. The functioning of a forgetful model is just like this modified “cups and ball” game. Denote by x

the list of swappings and xr the correct answer. Given a forgetful latent Pf , the likelihood of xr is given by (5.8): (Proj # Pf )( xr |x → Σ∗

> □

) =

Thus, the above argument indicates that forgetful models cannot approximate the compositional function (E.1), and therefore cannot approximate the hidden Markov model constructed in the proof of Theorem 2.1. Hence, we arrive at an informal proof that PHMM is not contained in the following subspace

and Pforget is not obvious, and it seems neither can approximate the other. Hence, the use of persistent and ubiquitous thinking is justified by its enhancement of approximation ability. Remark 5.2. Certainly one may strengthen a forgetful model by allowing the length bound c to grow with input length |x|, as in Example 2.3. The resulting models might be as expressive as the persistent thinking models, as the former can be seen as a modification of the latter that postpones all thoughts to the end of the sequence z ∈ Proj −1 

> <ω

(x). Even if this may work in theory, its implementation would be highly costly. Given a persistent thinking model, let Z ∈ Q∗(·| x) be its latent sample, and express it as Z = ( Y (t)xt)|x|

> t=1

. Inside the computation of the encoding of Z, the output logits at each token 

xT may depend on any of the previous thoughts ( Y (t))Tt=1 . In order to obtain the same output from a forgetful latent with function parameter fforget , in the worst case fforget needs to take the permuted sequence x<T Y (1) . . . Y (T )xT as input, and this encoding needs to be performed separately for each 

T = 1 , . . . |x|, resulting in an enormous cost. Therefore, it could be much more efficient to use a persistent thinking model than a forgetful one to achieve a given level of expressivity. 58 Proof. 5.6.2 Inference of the Explanatory Sampler 

Next, we briefly describe how to construct an explanatory sampler Q ∈ Q Proj . Since the latent P can now be supported on the entire Dom(Proj), the latent sequences can range in all of Proj −1 

> <ω

(x). Since the sampler is explanatory, each inserted thought Y (t) in a sampled latent sequence Z ∼ Q(·| x) can depend on all of the input x.Here is one straightforward implementation. Given any text x ∈ Σ∗, the format can simply be “x⟨explain ⟩Z”, such that the delimiter ⟨explain ⟩ can be either a reserved token or a short instruction, “x⟨explain ⟩” is the prompt for the sampler Q, and Z is the decoded output. This format allows each token in Z to depend on all of x. If we need to distinguish between the training and inference samplers, then a tag can be included as in Figure 5.2. To ensure that Z belongs to Proj −1 

> <ω

(x), the sampler should output in the format of Z = ( Y (t)xt)|x|

> t=1

, where each thought Y (t) is either empty or belongs to T .This can be achieved with the logit masking of Definition 2.8 and the next-token set Φ ≤1(z) from (2.26). In the current setting, Φ ≤1(z) has the following form: Let z ∈ Ω∗ be the current decoding and

– Else, set Φ ≤1(z) = {⟨ /s ⟩} 

• Else if a = ⟨/s ⟩

Considering that decoding is generally slower than encoding for LLMs, the above decoding process can be accelerated, especially when the nonempty thoughts are sparse. If the next token to be encoded by the autoregressive latent model Pf is either some xt+1 or ⟨/s ⟩, then we additionally encode some subsequent observable tokens, so the input becomes a chunk xt+1 . . . x t+C or ⟨/s ⟩xt+1 . . . x t+C , for some length C, which can be fixed or adaptively adjusted. Then, C softmax distributions are produced (the output distribution at ⟨/s ⟩ is a Dirac mass and can be ignored), each is binomial over two tokens 

{xt+i, ⟨s⟩} for i = 1 , . . . C , and we sample all of them; if none produces ⟨s⟩, then we proceed to encode the next chunk; else, we discard all the encoding after the first ⟨s⟩ and proceed to decoding the next thought. 

5.6.3 A Priori Balancing of Fast and Slow Thinking 

Finally, we design the training objective. The goal is to optimize under the speed-accuracy tradeoff, such that the model is able to minimize the cross entropy loss while keeping the density and lengths of the inserted thoughts under control. Instead of using ad hoc penalties, constraints or workflows [46, 122, 76, 173], we derive a training loss directly from the unified objective (4.2). Recall that Proj admits latent sequences z ∈ Proj −1 

> <ω

(x) with varying lengths. For any text x ∈ Σ∗,the shortest latent sequence is simply z = x, while the longest ones are z = ( y(t)xt)|x| 

> t=1

with |y(t)| =

c + 2 = 32770, such that a thought with maximum length is inserted before each token. In the worst case, even a short input text can be lifted to a long novel. Thus, from a cost perspective, it is of great interest whether a given latent distribution P ∈ P (Dom(Proj)) favors long latent sequences, as measured by the following average length ¯l(x) := EZ∼Q∗(·| x)[|Z|] = 

P  

> z∈Proj −1
> <ω (x)

|z| · P ≤| z|(z)(Proj # P )≤| x|(x)where Q∗ is the posterior sampler of this P .Typical training objectives, such as cross entropy (3.15), encourage the latent sequences Z ∼ Q∗(·| x)to grow as lengthy as possible. As exemplified by the comparison of Ppoly and Psimple , approximation ability is proportional to the amount of latent sequences, which in this case is proportional to the average length ¯l(x). Thus, for a model Proj # P to fit any target P∗ well, its latent length ¯l would tend to increase to the maximum allowed, which is 32771 · | x|. This is in accordance with the empirical observations that slow thinking models tend to overthink during inference [27, 168], and also their thought lengths keep growing monotonically during training [69, 182]. To avoid the enormous cost due to overly lengthy thoughts, a cost-aware training objective is needed. The quick fixes used in practice are rather artificial; for instance, if one tries to control ¯l(x) by adding a length penalty [46, 102], the choice of its coefficient and schedule cannot be determined a priori and are left to trial-and-error. 60 In principle, the training objective should be (4.2) that maximizes the rate of loss reduction with respect to the real time. Section 4 has argued that this objective can compel the model to generate long thoughts only when needed, similar to how humans balance their fast and slow thinking. To make the abstract objective (4.2) implementable, we derive its simplified form. Suppose the time cost for processing a length-l latent sequence during a training step is given by some function 

τ (l). Here, the processing includes the decoding, encoding and backpropagation of this sequence, and this τ can be obtained either by actual measurement or calculation. Considering that in practice the time cost is mainly due to decoding, specifically the loading of model weights from VRAM to SRAM (i.e. the memory wall) that is repeated for each decoded token, one may adopt the approximation 

τ (l) ≈ Cdecode l (and this coefficient can be ignored). Suppose for simplicity that the sampling size n

is fixed. Denote the mini-batch at each step s by {X(s,b )}Bb=1 , and the sampled latent sequences for training the latent model and sampler model(s) by {Z(s,b,i )}ni=1 . Then, the term ts+1 − ts on the right side of (4.2) can be modeled by the random variable 1

Still, it should be generally helpful to implement some form of future looking beyond the greedy approach to better approximate the total cost, e.g. using a value function (with respect to step s), finite-horizon optimization [132], or more general optimal control techniques [96]. The second approximation is to estimate the two terms of C (4.1), in particular the entropy H(P∗). One estimator is based on LLM likelihoods. Suppose we have a series of LLMs of increasing sizes that are trained on data sampled from P∗, and suppose there is a test dataset Xtest that is roughly a set of i.i.d. samples of P∗ and that has never been trained on by this LLM series. One may either prepare these models and Xtest by oneself, or use one of the popular LLM families [176, 175, 67] and let Xtest 

be texts that are written after the publication of these LLMs. Then, one computes the cross entropy of each LLM on Xtest , and fits a power-law curve f (m) = km −α + h between the model sizes and these cross entropies. The value f (∞) = h is an estimator of H(P∗). The mechanism is that, by Proposition H.1, the cross entropy equals H(P∗) plus some KL divergence, and one may assume that the latter goes to zero as the model size goes to infinity. Alternatively, one can consider classical estimators such as ones that are based on string matching and compression [55, 174]. Denote the estimated value of 

H(P∗) by ˜H(P∗). If only one sampler is used, then the cross-entropy in (4.1) can be estimated by the mini-batch loss Ln from (3.17), now with the samples {X(s,b )}Bb=1 , and C(θ) can be estimated by 

Cs(θ) = Ln(θ) − ˜H(P∗)If both the inference and train samplers are used, then C(θ) can be estimated by

The consideration is that controlling the latent length ¯l is only an objective for P , while the goal of the sampler(s) is always to fit the posterior sampler (and inquisitive sampler). Most of the road map (Figure 0.2) has been derived in Sections 5.5 and 5.6, except the entry “active lifting”, which we discuss now. So far our modeling uses a static format. The routine is always to start from a manually designed projection Proj, derive its lifting Proj −1 

> <ω

and sampler space QProj , and then train the latent model 

P and sampler Q (and Q ). That is why we use the term “static lifting” in Figure 0.2. While this approach to slow thinking is quite versatile (considering the families Psimple and Ppersist in the representation hierarchy), humans are more active thinkers. We were not taught what format we should perform slow thinking with (at least our ancestors did not have this luxury), but invented the various ways of thinking on our own. So it is natural to consider a generalized model with more degrees of freedom, which can develop into the projected distribution Proj # P and sampling-based inference, as well as many other possible forms. In a narrow sense, the purpose is to enhance the expressivity of the model and go further on the representation hierarchy. In a broad sense, a first-principles modeling of the agency of slow thinking may enable the model to invent concepts, schema and languages in an unsupervised manner. While a detailed plan is provided in Section 6 (in particular Section 6.6), the main idea is simple. We have seen in Section 4 that the unified objective (4.2) is sufficiently general such that its optimization leads to the maximization of both approximation ability and compute efficiency. Thus, this objective should also be capable of inducing a projection from scratch. To design a model space that includes the projections as a subset and supports end-to-end training, we let the latent samplers be the free variable, such that they can map to distributions over any latent sequences, unconstrained by Proj −1 

> <ω

.Then, it can be shown that if a sampler is an optimizer for the unified objective, then it must be restrict to the inverse sets Proj −1(x) of some function Proj (cf. Remark 6.1). Thus, a projection implicitly emerges, and the latent samplers can be seen as an active version of lifting. This approach seems very general, so Section 6 considers the modeling of data from all modalities. Both training and inference are discussed for this long-term plan. 

# 6 The Agency of Perceptual Representation 

Finally, we introduce the general theory of active lifting. The technical goal is to generalize the distribution representation Proj # P (and Problem 2) to a more active model without a prescribed projection. The broader goal is a universal approach to constructing encoders and generative models for all data modalities, such that they are able to induce concepts and schema in their latent sequences in a completely unsupervised fashion. This section tries to capture mathematically the intuition of “agency and urgency” from Example 1.3. Regarding agency, our modeling endows encoders and generative models with an internal time axis, and in particular an encoder needs to search for reasonable “understandings”. Regarding urgency, a training loss, which resembles minimum-length coding compression, is derived from the unified objective of Section 4. We also discuss the possibility of unifying the three existing approaches to image modeling, which are patch autoregression, diffusion, and the classical objects-and-parts representation. Still, the present discussion is purely theoretical, and the model design and experimental verification are left for future work. The basic idea is that a latent sequence z ∈ Ω∗ can serve as a description of some data sample x.As z grows longer, more information is specified, and eventually an infinite sequence z ∈ Ωω uniquely pins down this x. The act of perceiving can be modeled as the decoding of latent sequences, and the act of understanding as the search for latent sequences with high prior probability P ≤| z|(z), where P

is the marginal distribution of latent sequences (or the prior knowledge). The decoding steps give rise to the temporal dimension of perception that is independent of the “spatial” dimension of the data. Suppose one is impatient and wants to eliminate as quickly as possible the uncertainty of the scene that one is looking at (e.g. the capybara in Example 1.3). Then, each decoded token should carry as much information as possible. At the same time, these sequences should be understandable for one’s mind, i.e. can be approximated by a latent distribution model P . So one needs to invent one’s own “mental language” to optimize for both the efficiency and regularity of latent descriptions. 63 6.1 General Setup of Latent Sampling 

To begin with, this section defines latent sampling for data distributions of any modality. Let X be any topological space, and we call each element x ∈ X a data sample. Let P(X ) be the space of Borel probability measures and denote the target distribution by P∗ ∈ P (X ). This is a very general setting and includes all modalities that we know of. Later our examples will focus on texts and images: 

• Text: Xtext = Σ ∗ given a finite set (or vocabulary) Σ. This is the setting of our previous sections. 

• Image: Ximage can be defined as the set of measureable functions from [0 , 1] 2 to [0 , 1] 3, where [0 , 1] 2 and [0 , 1] 3 represent the two-dimensional image domain and the RGB color space. It may use the weak-∗ topology of L∞ or the topology induced by some perceptually realistic metric based on [83, 107]. Note that we are not restricted to the coarse grid of square patches that is commonly used in present models [44, 21]. One can similarity define the spaces for videos, graphs, molecules, and so on. Let Ω be an arbitrary finite set (with size at least 2), and we refer to Ω ω with the product topology as the latent space. We define latent samplers as follows. 

Definition 6.1. A sampler is a measureable function Q : X × Ω∗ → P (Ω). Namely, for each x ∈ X 

and z ∈ Ω∗, we have a conditional distribution Q(·| x, z ) over Ω. The space of samplers is denoted by 

Q+1 .Given any sampler Q ∈ Q +1 and length T ∈ N, a sampler over Ω T can be constructed by the autoregressive decoding of Q:

The sampler Q≤t can be interpreted as a probabilistic encoder that maps the observed data x to a length-t description Z ∼ Q≤t(·| x). The next section will derive a training objective, which might capture our intuition of a good description. For instance, if x is an image, the sample Z from a well-trained sampler would be an accurate and concise description of its contents; if x is a difficult text, Z would be an understanding that fully explains its logic. The marginal distribution Q≤t 

> ∗

> ∗

(z) would be near zero, as it is very unlikely that an image X ∼ P∗

can induce this z (except in “Tom & Jerry”). The challenge is that we cannot assume a priori the existence of any language, and the prior Q≤ω

> ∗

needs to evolve into a language from scratch. By language, we mean an assignment of token sequences to data X ∼ P∗ that is both efficient (a short description can roughly pin down X) and regular (learnable by parametrized functions such as Transformers). Perhaps this process can be compared to how our human ancestors developed perceptual concepts and ultimately invented natural languages. Even if the data are Xtext , the latent sequences z ∈ Ω≤ω are not necessarily in the same language; instead, they are more likely to resemble the mental languages that form our thoughts [133, 58]. While efficiency follows directly from the unified objective (Section 4), regularity is the result of fitting Q≤ω

> ∗

with a latent distribution model P . After all, unlike Q≤t, the prior Q≤t 

> ∗

is not directly accessible and needs to be approximated. We will see that training can proceed as long as we have the sampler and latent distribution, but one can optionally model the posterior data distribution P∗(·| z). By (6.2), this provides a generative model for P∗.Hence, the static Problem 2 can be generalized as follows, in a slightly informal fashion.

## 6.2 Active Lifting 

This section derives from the unified objective (Section 4) the training loss for the latent sampler 

Q ∈ Q +1 and latent distribution P ∈ P (Ω ω ), as a characterization of the notion of urgency from Example 1.3. 65 Recall that the unified objective (4.2) has the form R ∞ 

> 0

Ctdt , where t is the elapsed time and C

is a measure of “reducible uncertainty”, so the objective maximizes the rate of uncertainty reduction over the real time. For language models, this reduction happens across the training steps (as in Sections 4 and 5.6.3), but now we consider the observation steps instead. By observation, we mean the autoregressive decoding process Zt ∼ Q(·| x, Z <t ) given any data x. As a coarse approximation, we assume that the tokens Zt are decoded at a constant rate, so the elapsed time can be equated with the observation step. To define the “reducible uncertainty” of the observation process, let us consider for any data x and any observation step t ∈ N ∪ { ω}, the posterior data distribution P∗(·| Z) from (6.2) with Z sampled from Q≤t(·| x). Intuitively, it represents the uncertainty of the observer after looking at x for t moments. A description Z has been obtained, but it is insufficient to uniquely determine 

x, leaving a posterior distribution of possible data whose descriptions may start with Z. The tree of possibilities in Figure 1.3 can be interpreted in this way, e.g. after the first second, the decoded token 

Z1 carries the meaning of “a fluffy creature”, and P∗(·| Z1) is the distribution of a lot of mammals, as depicted by the level 1 sub-tree in Figure 1.3. A natural measure of uncertainty would be the negative log-likelihood − log P∗(x|Z). The technical issue is that, since the space X could be uncountable, we should use the density function of P∗(·| Z)instead of the probability values. Since X in general does not have a “natural” base measure (such as the Lebesgue measure for Rd), we need to choose a reference measure to define the density function. With the reference measure set to P∗, we define the density of P∗(·| Z) as the Radon-Nikodym derivative

## 6.3 Minimum-Length Regular Coding 

This section analyzes the training objective (6.7), showing that it resembles compression, specifically minimum-length coding with some regularity constraint. If we characterize “language” (at least de-scriptive language) as an assignment of descriptions to data samples that is both efficient and learnable, then this objective compels the model to invent a language on its own. First, we establish the connection to minimum-length coding. Let us consider the simple setting when Q and P can range in all of Q+1 and P(Ω ω ), instead of being constrained to any parametrized families. Suppose ( Q, P ) is a global optimizer, then P always equals the prior Q≤ω 

> ∗

. Assume for simplicity that X is a countable set. Then, we express Ct in terms of (6.5) and omit the constant term 

H(P∗). The objective (6.7) is equivalent to min  

> Q≤ω∈Q ω

− log P (x|z≤t)dQ ≤ω (z|x)dP ∗(x) (6.10) where Qω denotes the space of measureable functions from X to P(Ω ω ). In terms of the interpretations in Section 4, we are maximizing the reduction rate of conditional entropy. A classic problem in information theory is entropy coding, or the minimization of expected code length [137, 79]. A particular version of this problem is that, given any countable set X , distribution 

P∗ ∈ P (X ) and finite vocabulary Ω ( |Ω| > 1), construct a coding function c to minimize the expected length EX∼P∗ [|c(X)|]. A coding is defined as an injective function c : X → Ω∗ whose image set c(X )is prefix-free. Shannon’s source coding theorem implies that the expected length is lower bounded by entropy EX∼P∗ [− log |Ω| P∗(X)]. An example of an (approximately) optimal coding based on Huffman tree is given by Figure 6.1 (left). Note that with any coding c we can make the following approximation 

|c(X)| =

− log P∗(X | z(X)≤t) (6.11) where z(x) = c(x)⟨blank ⟩ω

∀z′ ∈ Ω∗, P∗(·| z′) = law( X | z′ ⊑ z(X)) The coding z(X) is c(X) padded with some blank symbol ⟨blank ⟩ /∈ Ω so that the prefix z(X)≤t can be defined for all t. The intuition of this approximation is that for t < |c(X)|, there are possibly other x′

with the same prefix c(X)≤t = c(x′)≤t, which make P∗(X|c(X)≤t) < 1, while for t ≥ | c(x)|, we always have P∗(X|c(X)≤t) = 1. Thus, 1t< |c(X)| can be seen as the binarized version of − log P∗(X | z(X)≤t). Figure 6.1 (middle) illustrates the positive correlation between the two sides of (6.11). It follows that

Note that our training objective (6.10) highly resembles (6.12), as the sampling distribution Q≤ω (·| x)can be seen as a stochastic generalization of the coding z(x). Hence, one may claim that the unified objective (4.2) induces minimum-length coding when perception is modeled by latent sampling. For concreteness, consider a distribution P∗ of photographs. Since the objects are the recurring patterns in photographs, they are the most distinctive features from a minimum-length coding perspective. Thus, if a sampler Q is trained on P∗, one may expected that its latent tokens Zt will encode these objects and their parts, attributes, relations, etc. A slightly more rigorous discussion will be provided 68 Figure 6.1: Illustration of the resemblance between minimum-length coding and our training objective. Suppose Ω = {0, 1}. Left: The Huffman tree for some distribution P∗ over a set X with size |X | = 20. It is approximately the minimum-length coding of P∗. Each leaf (red node) represents a code c(x), with its probability P∗(x) annotated underneath. Middle: A scatter plot depicting the positive correlation (6.11). The two axes are the two sides of (6.11), with each x ∈ X shown as a point. These quantities are computed based on the distribution and coding of the left panel. Right: A scatter plot depicting the positive correlation (6.12). For each N ∈ { 10 , . . . 1000 }, a distribution P∗ over N elements is randomly generated by uniformly sampling in [0 , 1] N and then normalizing by the sum. Then, the Huffman coding c for P∗ is obtained, and the two sides of (6.12) are computed. The color gradient indicates the increasing of N .in Section 6.7. Creating concepts from unsupervised perceptual data is known as concept induction [75, 91], and this training objective may be sufficient for the sampler to induce concepts. Next, we consider regularity. Without loss of generality, suppose the latent distribution P is a Transformer LLM, i.e. P ∈ P plain from Section 2.9. Denote P = Pf with function parameter f . By definition of a global optimizer, an optimal sampler Q must ensure that its marginal distribution Q≤ω

> ∗

is achievable (or at least approximable) by Pf . Thus, Theorem 2.1 (and Figure 2.1) implies that Q≤ω

> ∗

cannot involve a too complicated dependency structure. For instance, if P∗ is a distribution of images of book pages, and each latent token zt encodes a printed word, then a sampled sequence Z ∼ Q≤ω

> ∗

corresponds to some arrangement of the words in a book page. In order for Z to be easily readable by 

Pf , the sampler Q needs to arrange these words in the usual linear order, instead of some arbitrary order. Similarly, if P∗ consists of photographs of daily-life scenes, and each zt encodes a visual concept, then it might be more tractable for Pf to have these concepts presented in the SVO format (subject-verb-object, such as “the face contains two eyes”) instead of some random VSSOOVOSO. . . . Hence, the training objective not only induces perceptual concepts in Q≤ω 

> ∗

, but also forces Q≤ω 

> ∗

to organize these concepts in some learnable format, grammar, or schema. 

## 6.4 New Approach to Generative Modeling 

This section discusses how to turn the posterior data distribution P∗(·| z) into a generative model. Informally, since the equation (6.2) has only one “degree of freedom” and the sampler Q is a free variable, it should be straightforward to obtain P∗(·| z), just like fitting Q≤ω 

> ∗

with a latent model P .This task is optional, as the objective (6.7) only requires Q and P .Generally speaking, given a target distribution P∗, a generative model is a solution to 

Z

G(·| z)dP (z) = P∗

consisting of a latent distribution P and a conditional distribution G that are both easy to sample from. This G is commonly called the generator. Then, one can sample from P∗ by 

X ∼ G(·| Z), Z ∼ P (6.13) 69 Often, P is set to be unit Gaussian, and G is simplified to a measureable function Proj to solve for Proj # P = P∗. One of the greatest difficulties for designing a generative model is the non-uniqueness of the solution [178]. The classical approach, adopted by models such as the normalizing flows [153], variational autoencoder [94] and generative adversarial networks [66], is to allow for any solution, so their loss landscapes are highly nonconvex and they are prone to training failure [178, Sections 6.3-6.4]. An alternative approach, adopted by models such as diffusion [143] and flow matching [3], is to adopt the trivial solution G(·| z) ≡ P∗ and try to extend it to some stochastic path whose velocity field can be expressed as an expectation over P∗, thus converting the problem to regression. Although these two approaches enjoy empirical success, it remains a question of theoretical interest whether one can construct, a priori , a non-trivial yet tractable target P∗(·| z) for the generator to fit. Such a target P∗(·| z) can be obtained from our modeling. Recall from Remark 6.1 that the posterior data distribution P ≤t 

> ∗

for each t ∈ N+ ∪ { ω} tends to be deterministic, as a result of minimizing the general objective P∞ 

> t=0

Ct. So P ≤t 

> ∗

is less trivial than G(·| z) ≡ P∗. Besides, Section 6.3 suggests that the latent distribution Q≤ω 

> ∗

might resemble a descriptive language with concepts and grammar, so P ≤t

> ∗

can be seen as an artist/writer that creates an image/text based on its description. Thus, P ≤t 

> ∗

is a non-trivial conditional distribution. Meanwhile, P ≤t 

> ∗

is implementable, as we can train a generator to fit it with regression. Suppose the generator G is a measureable function Ω ≤ω → P (X ) and its likelihood G(x|z) is well-defined. Denote by G≤t the restriction of G to Ω t. Given any sampler Q, for each t we have 

EZ∼Q≤t

> ∗

h

KL  P ≤t 

> ∗

(·| Z) G≤t(·| Z)i

= EX∼P∗ EZ∼Q≤t(·| X)

 − log G≤t(X|Z) + log P ≤t 

> ∗

(X|Z) (6.14) = EX∼P∗ EZ∼Q≤t(·| X)

 − log G≤t(X|Z) + constant (6.15) The last line is straightforward to carry out as we only train G.Hence, the training objective (6.7) may lead to a new approach to generative modeling. The aforementioned two approaches (unprescribed solution and trivial solution) are sometimes called the “free coupling” and “product coupling” [178], where coupling means a joint distribution whose two marginal distributions are P and P∗. Thus, one may refer to this new approach as the “linguistic coupling”, given that Section 6.3 has compared active lifting to descriptive languages.

## 6.5 The Time Axis of Inference 

Having discussed training, we move on to inference. This section characterizes the agency of perception, equipping encoders with a time axis. As studied in Section 3, inference involves the four tasks of {unconditional, conditional }×{ encoding, generation }, where conditional means P∗ conditioned on some event (or measureable subset) A. This section studies unconditional inference, while conditional inference is discussed in the next section, with a focus on text data. Since unconditional generation is straightforward by (6.13), we consider unconditional encoding, namely estimating the probabilities P∗(x) given any input x ∈ X . Suppose X

## 6.6 Adaptation to Text Data 

To show that the Stage Three plan for improving slow thinking models (Section 5.7) is feasible, we apply the framework of active lifting to text data. While unconditional inference can be implemented as in Section 6.5 for general modalities, conditional inference (e.g. decoding given a prompt) is the main theme of the text modality and requires a bit more design. The method introduced in this section is probably applicable to other modalities as well, such as video generation given a prompt and image inpainting. While it should be doable to allow our modeled distribution Pmodel to range in P(Σ ω ) as in our static theory, for active lifting the sampler Q≤ω does not a priori obey causality in the sense of Remark 2.7, which would complicate our analysis. Thus, for convenience, let both Pmodel and the target distribution P∗ be supported over finite-length texts Xtext = Σ ∗, and moreover, suppose that 71 their supports are prefix-free (e.g. each sequence contains exactly one ⟨eos ⟩ that is placed at the end). Suppose we have performed the training processes of Sections 6.2 and 6.4 and obtained a well-trained sampler Q, latent model P and generator G, such that P = Q≤ω 

> ∗

and G(·| z) = P∗(·| z) for all z ∈ Ωω .It follows that P∗ = Pmodel = R G(·| z)dP (z). Furthermore, as discussed in Remark 6.1, it is reasonable to assume that ( Q≤ω , G ) forms an autoencoder in the sense of (6.9), namely 

Z

G(·| z)dQ ≤ω (z|x) = δx

for P∗-almost all x. As an abuse of notation, denote [ x] = {x′ ∈ Σ∗ | x ⊆ x′}.Given any prompt x ∈ Σ∗ such that P∗([ x]) > 0, conditional decoding means sampling from the conditional distribution P∗(·| [x]). To solve this, note that

Q≤ω (·| x) = Q≤ω<ω (·| x). Thus, it is natural to use Q≤ω (·| x) to approximate Q≤ω<ω (·| x). The training loss can simply be KL-divergence

For implementation, one can either train a separate copy of the underlying model of Q in order not to alter the sampler Q≤ω , or use the same model and include this loss in the training process of active lifting. Then, suppose that Q has been trained on (6.19) such that Q≤ω ≈ Q≤ω<ω . Conditional decoding can be performed based on the algorithms in Section 3.3. Analogous to Proposition 3.3, one can show that the decoding algorithm (6.21) enjoys a small error that goes to 0 as n → ∞ ,

Conditional encoding can be similarly performed. Given any nonempty texts xq , x r ∈ Σ∗ (e.g. a query and a response) such that xq xr ∈ sprt P∗, we can evaluate the conditional probability

∼ Q≤ω (·| xq xr ), { ˜Z(i)}ni=1 i.i.d.

∼ Q≤ω (·| xq )One can check that Q≤ω<ω is the variance-minimizing sampler (or posterior sampler) for the denominator. For the practical implementation of training and inference, one would need to apply a finite-length cutoff to all sampled latent sequences Z ∼ Q≤ω (·| x). In theory, the minimum-length coding analogy in Section 6.3 suggests that the entries Zt ceases to provide more information when t exceeds the minimum code length of x, so it suffices to keep a finite prefix of Z. In practice, one can either choose some large and uniform cutoff length T or implement an adaptive cutoff in the spirit of Section 5.6.3. With all sequences being finite, one may implement all three components Q, P, G by one underlying LLM: For instance, when playing Q, the LLM takes in the prompt x⟨s⟩ and decodes some z; when playing P , the LLM simply encodes z; when playing G, the LLM takes in z⟨/s ⟩x and decodes some 

X′, which is a continuation of x. Namely, X = xX ′ is a sample of P∗(·| [x]). In summary, an active lifting model is specified by the tuple ( X , Ω, Q, P, G ), with data space 

X , latent vocabulary Ω, sampler Q ∈ Q +1 , latent model P ∈ P (Ω ω ), and optionally, generator 

G : Ω ≤ω → P (X ). Regarding inference, unconditional encoding can be performed by either (6.17) or (6.18), conditional encoding by (6.22), unconditional generation by (6.13), and conditional generation by (6.20) or (6.21). Regarding training, one can either train Q, P by (6.7) to obtain an encoder and then train G separately by (6.15) to obtain a generative model, or train all three together by (6.16), which resembles an autoencoder. Optionally, the sampler loss (6.19) can be included to enable conditional inference. 

Remark 6.3. Apparently the modelings introduced in this paper rely heavily on sequences; the latent space Z = Ω ω always consists of sequences, and for the majority of our sections, the data space 

X = Σ ω is also sequences. From a measure theoretic point of view, this is a very general setup, since any uncountable Polish space is Borel isomorphic to {0, 1}ω , so whether the observable or latent data are sequences is mostly a matter of perspective. Yet, perspective does matter here, as we need a “perspective” that allows the existence of a lifted distribution that can be realized by neural networks (cf. Problem 3), preferably with small complexity (cf. Section 2.1). Though sufficiently abstract for a first-principles theory, the choice Z = Ω ω is more like an “axiom” instead of a derived result, and reflects the dominance of sequence models (specifically Transformers) in machine learning today. Meanwhile, regarding X , in particular those of non-sequence modalities, our modeling can be described informally as equipping any data with a sequence structure, so that we can perceive/generate they like reading/writing texts. Still, for now, only Sections 4 and Section 6 (except Section 6.6) are directly applicable to non-sequence data. 

## 6.7 Unification of Existing Representations 

This section is an informal study of the feature representation of the sampler Q, with a focus on image data. One purpose is to make the general discussion in Section 6.3 on concept induction and language invention more concrete. A visualization method called “visual field trajectory” is introduced. (However, we emphasize that this diagnostic tool is not part of our model’s architecture or training, nor is it based on biological modeling.) We discuss the relation of our model to the existing computer vision models, and the possibility of unifying their representations. Let ( Q, P ) be a global optimizer of the training objective (6.7) with the latent distribution P =

Q≤ω 

> ∗

belonging to some parametrized family such as Pplain . Based on Remark 6.1, we may assume that P ≤ω 

> ∗

is deterministic (concentrated on some measureable function Proj). Then, it is reasonable to assume that for P -almost all z ∈ Ωω , the posterior distribution P ≤t 

> ∗

(·| z≤t) converges weakly to 

P ≤ω 

> ∗

(·| z) = δProj( z). Consider X = Ximage . Given a random image X ∼ P∗, let Z ∼ Q≤ω (·| X) be a latent sample. Define the pointwise variances over the image domain u ∈ [0 , 1] 2,

∀t ∈ N, σ2

> ∗

∀t ∈ N+, Ft(u) =  σt−1(u) − σt(u)

. Conceptually, the introduction of Zt makes the support of Ft less uncertain, so this is probably the region that the model extracts information from (or whose state the model can infer) at the t-th observation step. Since P ≤0

u ∈ [0 , 1] 2 will eventually be attended to by sufficiently many Zt’s to become fully determined. Besides the spatial domain, one can also consider the frequency domain and define the visual fields with the Fourier basis 

∀ξ ∈ Z2, σfreq

> [0 ,1] 2

(ξ) =  σfreq

> t

(ξ)

> +

Based on the analogy of minimum-length coding in Section 6.3, the tokens Zt may correspond to the most distinctive features that can rapidly pin down the image X, distinguishing it from the rest of 

P∗. So their visual fields Ft tend to overlap with the visual patterns that are recurring throughout P∗.For instance, if P∗ consists of images of daily-life scenes, then each Ft could cover an object or one of its constituent parts, and if P∗ consists of images of book pages, then each Ft could be a printed word, phrase or sentence. Moreover, based on the notion of regularity discussed in Section 6.3, the visual field trajectory ( Ft)∞ 

> t=1

tends to be an understandable ordering of these regions, so that the marginal distribution of these trajectories has a simple dependency structure. For instance, the model would read the printed words in the the raster order (reading from left to right, from top to bottom) instead of some arbitrary order, and would look first at the entirety of a person, then the face and clothes, then the detailed facial features and so on, instead of jumping abruptly from this person’s ears to the other person’s shoes. This ordering of concepts gives rise to visual schema. Thus, the training objective (6.7) compels the visual representation of our model to adapt to the characteristics of the image distribution. Below are three more detailed examples that relate this representation to those of several existing models in computer vision. 

Example 6.1 (Text reading and patch autoregression) . Following the above discussion, if P∗ involves book pages and papers, then the sampler Q would parse X in the raster order, so that the latent model P can read these texts in the linear order. If we compare the visual fields Ft to the square patches commonly used in computer vision, then their trajectory ( Ft)t correspondes to the popular representation of patch autoregression [156, 157, 150], so the latter can be seen as a special case of our representation. Moreover, Q possesses the degree of freedom to read like humans, such as skimming through sentences with low information content (i.e. Ft becomes a large region) and reexamining an earlier paragraph for new insights. 

Example 6.2 (Diffusion) . P∗ could be spatially homogeneous images that exhibit more salient char-acteristics in the frequency domain, such as the images of textures, forests, and starry skies. It could be simpler to model them autoregressively in the Fourier space, with each visual field σfreq  

> t

being con-centrated at some frequency ξt ∈ Z2. The visual field trajectory imposes an ordering on the frequencies 

{ξt}∞

> t=1

, such as by increasing magnitude ∥ξt∥. Since the well-known diffusion models [141, 142, 134] can be interpreted as autoregressive models in the frequency domain (in either the default low-to-high order or some manually designed order) [40, 52], one may conjecture that these models are also a special case of our modeling. 

Example 6.3 (Objects and parts) . Images of daily-life scenes have multiscale compositional contents, which means that each image may consist of multiple objects, each object may consist of multiple parts, 74 and so on, and that they may possess multiple attributes and relations. In pursuit of this human-like representation, part-based modeling [53, 54, 57] was a popular research topic in computer vision before deep learning became dominant. Even now, people often assess the quality of deep vision models by how closely their learned features resemble the real objects and parts [21, 185, 70]. Our preceding discussions indicate that with a well-trained latent sampler Q, the latent tokens Zt may acquire the role of visual concepts, and the subsequences of Z ∼ Q≤ω may serve as visual schema. These visual schema enjoy both the efficiency of part-based representations and the flexibility of deep vision models. It is conceptually possible for Q to go beyond manually labeled concepts and schema, discovering all possible attributes (“having a rough surface attractive to cats”), relations (“caught goofing off by” could be a relation between an employee and an employer), compositions (“an umbrella hat”), and so on. These examples suggest that perhaps a vision model based on latent sequences can help to unify the apparently distinct vision models that people currently know of and establish a larger design space. Also, in accordance with [166, 29], Example 6.1 advocates for encoding texts with vision models, removing the need of tokenizers and allowing for more flexible reading orders. Meanwhile, Example 6.3 suggests that, since the latent sequences Z ∼ Q≤ω (·| x) resemble the visual concepts of humans, they might align well with text tokens, such that it is easier to integrate Q≤ω , as an image encoder, into vision-language models [2, 10, 100] compared to the existing image encoders. 

Remark 6.4 (Active reading and world model) . So far we have discussed how the objective of minimum-length regular coding induces the visual field trajectories to visit the contents of an im-age in an efficient yet understandable order. What if there are two images? For instance, x can be a photograph of two paintings, placed side by side. Minimum-length coding urges the visual field trajec-tory to visit the most distinctive parts of both paintings, probably switching frequently between them. Meanwhile, regularity prevents the trajectory from switching too rapidly, in order to obtain reasonable visual schema. This tradeoff compels the sampler Q to actively search for the best “itinerary” that moves at a moderate and adaptive pace. We can go further to consider an infinitely large “image” that contains infinitely many scenes (such as the internet or the physical world). An optimal itinerary for this “image” might be: First, visit the websites for learning languages; then, read all the interesting books in the world; finally, design and launch a spaceship to see the universe, etc. Hence, the combi-nation of the unified objective (4.2) and latent sampling might become a technical solution to active reading and world models. 

# 7 Preliminary Experiment 

The implementation of the numerous by-products of this paper are left for future work. Nevertheless, we perform a preliminary experiment to test the Stage One improvement of slow thinking from Section 5.5. The theory of active lifting holds as long as its derivations are valid, but experiments are helpful for demonstrating that the theory is implementable with the existing technology. Recall that the Stage One improvement substitutes the identity sampler with explanatory sam-pler(s) to improve the sampling efficiency of training. One consequence is that the model could make greater progress when trained with the same data and sampling size n, since the sampling errors in the training gradients are reduced. We verify this prediction, with progress measured by the reduction in test loss. We consider the simple setting when only the inference sampler Q is instantiated, which fits the posterior sampler Q∗. By Section 5.5, a model with an explanatory sampler would train its latent distribution on (5.20) and its sampler on (5.24). Putting them together, the latent model Pθ and sampler Qψ are trained simultaneously on the losses

It is fair for the control group to use the predictive sampler instead of the identity sampler, since the former is more general and makes the control group stronger (cf. Section 3.6). For convenience, Pθ and Qψ use separate copies of this LLM, instead of sharing one copy. One detail to be aware of is that usually the output logits of Transformer LLMs are modified by default during decoding [80], e.g. with lower temperature and top-k cutoff.
