# 8 Related Work 

Many related works have already been mentioned in this paper, and this section lists the remaining ones. 

Slow thinking Models: General discussions of the current status of slow thinking models are pro-vided by [190, 121]. Notable phenomenological studies of the mechanisms of slow thinking include [77, 109, 164]. One major problem of slow thinking is policy collapse [182, 35], and current solutions focus on token-wise operations that encourage exploration [78, 163, 28]. We identify a possible cause (Remark 3.8) and take the different approach of using the inquisitive sampler to reduce the sampling variance of the training gradient. Another problem of slow thinking is to avoid overthinking and reduce inference cost [149] and the engineering solutions include [191, 98, 42]. Section 5.6.3 takes the different approach of deriving an intrinsic loss function from the unified objective. Meanwhile, similar to our latent sampling, several works have studied slow thinking as a sampling problem [184, 89], notably with [192] that also discovered the posterior sampling distribution. Designs that resemble the explanatory sampler have been proposed in earlier works [101, 99], in the specific setting with question-answer data, though they are introduced as engineering heuristics instead of from a unified derivation. Some models perform slow thinking in the feature space, a method known as continuous chain-of-thought [26, 71], and it would be interesting to see if our framework with discrete latent spaces can be extended to the continuous setting. 

First-principles modeling: Theories that try to model intelligence at the fundamental level were proposed even in the early days of AI [169, 161, 162, 154]. More recent works include the pattern theory that models real-world data [68, 115], the free-energy principle that models how the brain minimizes surprise [59, 60], a derivation of animal behaviors from a progressive refinement of feedback control loops [31, 32], and a derivation of the Transformer architecture from compression [183, 19]. 

Latent variable models: Broadly speaking, our active lifting theory concerns the unsupervised dis-covery of meaningful latent variables that can explain a data distribution, which is a general topic 77 in machine learning. For Euclidean data, the classical methods include clustering and principle com-ponents [120, 6, 152]. For sequence data, besides HMMs, we have conditional random fields [95] and probabilistic context-free grammar [33]. Prior to deep learning, latent structure models were popular in natural language processing, whose structures include parse trees [16], document topics [16], bilin-gual word correspondences [18], etc. Similarly, information-theoretic training objectives were devised to guide computer vision models to develop disentangled, semantically meaningful latent variables [25, 75]. 

Bounded-depth circuits: Our representation hierarchy largely depends on the characterization of Transformers as bounded-depth circuits. This boundedness has been studied by multiple works [112, 146, 24]. Besides slow thinking, other ways for LLMs to tackle this constraint include using recurrent layers [63, 71, 188], adopting the classical recurrent neural networks [14], and test-time updates [151, 125]. 

# 9 Conclusion 

This paper presents a mathematical theory called “active lifting”, as a first-principles modeling of the cognitive function of slow thinking, or more generally, active perception. The general theory starts from the sampling of latent sequences and the maximization of uncertainty reduction rate, and transforms data distributions to lifted distributions on a sequence space Ω ω to make them easier to model (Problems 1, 2 and 3). Thus, inference (or more generally, encoding) is endowed with an internal time axis, along which latent samplers produce sequences to “describe” the observations. Meanwhile, training is to construct a lifted distribution that is both regular (learnable by neural networks, or more generally, bounded-depth Boolean circuits) and efficient (analogous to the compression method of minimum-length coding), thus resembling the invention of descriptive languages. While active lifting implicitly develops a deterministic projection function, one can consider the sub-theory for text data with a prescribed projection Proj : Ω ω ⇀ Σω . This static theory leads to the representation hierarchy, a cascade of subspaces of the space of data distributions P(Σ ω ), and the sampler hierarchy, a cascade of subspaces of the sampler space QProj . The existing slow thinking models can be derived from the lower levels of the two hierarchies, while ascending these hierarchies makes a model more expressive (capable of modeling complex data distributions) and efficient (improving the performance of training and inference with fixed data and compute). For future work, one direction could be the first-principles modeling of the data distributions. It would be interesting to try to derive the familiar characteristics of natural language texts and natural images, without using any empirical knowledge of them. Such modeling could help to make the arguments of Section 6 more rigorous, e.g. defining the visual concepts and visual schema as well as their emergence during the training process of active lifting. In practice, one might try to recreate the process by which our human ancestors invented natural languages. Meanwhile, it could be useful to study the dynamics of the latent sampler along the perceptual time axis. While an indirect characterization is provided by our visual field trajectory, an in-depth analysis would be beneficial. One possible outcome could be a more formal formulation of the agency of perception, which might extend to the agency of behavior. The latter can help to characterize the design space of information-seeking behaviors (Figure 0.1), and thus implement the world model discussed in Remark 6.4.

# A Background on Circuit Complexity 

This section provides a brief introduction to the key concepts from circuit complexity theory that are used in this paper. Standard materials on Boolean circuits can be found in [160, 8, 145], while materials on DLOGTIME -uniform TC 0 circuits can be found in [13, 82]. 90 A Boolean circuit is a mapping {0, 1}n → { 0, 1}m, where the output length m defaults to 1 unless stated otherwise. It can be defined as a directed acyclic graph (DAG) with n sources (vertices without incoming edges) and m sinks (vertices without outgoing edges). Each non-source vertex is a gate, either AND, OR or NOT. Each AND or OR gate can have unbounded fan-in (incoming edges), and each non-sink gate can have unbounded fan-out (outgoing edges). The circuit size is the number of gates, and circuit depth the longest path in this DAG. A set {Cn}∞ 

> n=0

is called a circuit family if each 

Cn has n sources. A language is a subset of binary strings {0, 1}∗ = S∞

> n=0

{0, 1}n. For instance, PARITY consists of strings with an even number of 1’s. A circuit family {Cn}∞ 

> n=0

decides or recognizes a language L if for any string x, x belongs to L if and only if C|x|(x) = 1. So different circuits are responsible for inputs of different lengths. 

Definition A.1. An AC 0 circuit family {Cn} consists of bounded-depth polynomial-size Boolean circuits, i.e. whose circuit depths are O(1) in n and circuit sizes are O(nk) for some k ∈ N. AC 0

denotes the set of languages decidable by AC 0 circuit families. 

Definition A.2. An NC 1 circuit family {Cn} consists of polynomial-size Boolean circuits whose circuit depths are O(log n) and whose AND and OR gates all have fan-in = 2. NC 1 denotes the set of languages decidable by NC 1 circuit families. To avoid degenerate examples, some uniformity condition is usually imposed on circuit families. For instance, a circuit family {Cn} is P-uniform if there exists a polynomial-time Turing machine that maps each string 1 n to a description of Cn. It is known that the set of languages decidable by 

P-uniform circuit families is exactly the complexity class P [8]. 

Definition A.3. A circuit family {Cn} is DLOGTIME -uniform if there exists a deterministic Turing machine such that, given the input ( n, i, j ), it determines in O(log n) time the type of the i-th gate of 

Cn and whether ( i, j ) is a directed edge in Cn.In particular, it follows that a DLOGTIME -uniform circuit family must have a polynomial circuit size, since otherwise the gate numbers i, j cannot be read in O(log n) time. A threshold gate is a Boolean function {0, 1}n → { 0, 1} that outputs 1 if and only if ≥ k (or ≤ k)of its input bits are 1, for some n and k. Since the AND, OR and NOT gates can each be implemented by a threshold gate, a threshold circuit can be defined as a Boolean circuit all of whose gates are threshold gates. There are several other ways to define threshold circuits and they are equivalent up to some constant change in depth and polynomial change in size [65]. 

Definition A.4. A TC 0 circuit family consists of bounded-depth polynomial-size threshold circuits. 

TC 0 denotes the set of languages decidable by TC 0 circuit families. It is known that AC 0 ⊊ TC 0 ⊆ NC 1 [160]. The conjecture TC 0 ⊊ NC 1 is widely believed to be true [180, 5, 147]. 

DLOGTIME -uniform TC 0 circuits are of particular interest, as they can implement many arith-metical operations [73, 82]. In particular, each Transformer network can be modeled as a DLOGTIME -uniform TC 0 circuit family [30]. In order to model a function f : Ω ∗ → Σ∗, where Ω , Σ are arbitrary finite sets, some adaptations need to be made. First, since the inputs and outputs of Boolean circuits are binary, we represent each element of Ω (or Σ) as a binary string of length ⌈log 2 |Ω|⌉ (or ⌈log 2 |Σ|⌉ ), and thus speak of the input segment i⌈log 2 |Ω|⌉ , (i + 1) ⌈log 2 |Ω|⌉  as the i-th input token (and the i-th output token is similarly defined). Second, the circuits need to handle variable-length outputs. As each Ct models f restricted to Ω t, its output length has a finite upper bound bt = max z∈Σt |f (z)|, so we let Ct output bt tokens. Since some outputs may have length < b t, we include a ⟨blank ⟩ symbol and allow Ct to output  Σ ∪ {⟨ blank ⟩} bt

.Define the function strip :  Σ ∪ {⟨ blank ⟩} ∗ → Σ∗ that strips all occurrences of ⟨blank ⟩ from a string. Then, we say that {Ct} models f if strip ◦ Ct = f |Ωt for each t.With non-determinism, these circuits can be extended to model set-valued functions f : Σ ∗ → 2Ω∗

.

Definition A.5. A non-deterministic circuit C : {0, 1}n → 2{0,1}m

is a nonempty-set-valued function. It can be modeled by a circuit ˜C : {0, 1}n+k → { 0, 1}m with k auxiliary input bits for some k, and 

∀x ∈ { 0, 1}n, C(x) =  ˜C(x, y ) y ∈ { 0, 1}k

91 Hence, we say that a non-deterministic circuit family {Ct} models f if 

∀t, ∀x ∈ Σt, {strip( z) | z ∈ Ct(x)} = f (x)Note that if f : Ω ∗ → Σ∗ can be modeled by a DLOGTIME -uniform circuit family, then its output length is at most polynomial, |f (x)| = O(poly (|x|)). If f : Σ ∗ → 2Ω∗

can be modeled by a DLOGTIME -uniform nondeterministic circuit family, then the circuits can have at most O(poly (|x|)) auxiliary input bits, and thus the output set has size 2 O(poly (|x|)) .

C(Σ ω , R) with respect to the uniform metric. Hence, P1([ x]) = P2([ x]) (or Pn([ x]) → P∗([ x])) for all 

x ∈ Σ∗ is equivalent to R f dP 1 = R f dP 2 (or R f dP n → R f dP ∗) for all f ∈ C(Σ ω , R).

Proposition B.1. Given any measureable spaces A, B and a measureable partial function f : A ⇀ B ,1. If P(B) = {f #P | P ∈ P (A)} where P denotes the space of probability measures, then f is surjective. 2. Suppose A, B are Polish spaces (separable and completely metrizable), f is continuous, and its domain Dom( f ) is an Fσ set (a countable union of closed sets). If f is surjective, then P(B) = 

{f #P | P ∈ P (A)}, where P denotes the space of Borel probability measures. 

92 This proposition is relevant to our setting, since Σ ω , Ωω (with product topology) are Polish spaces, and for all examples considered in this paper the projections Proj are continuous and the domains Dom(Proj) are closed (and thus Fσ ). In fact, the condition of f being continuous might be unnecessary, if one applies [92, Exercise 13.5]. 

Proof of Proposition B.1. For the first statement, suppose f is not surjective. Choose some b ∈ B that does not belong to the image of f , and let δb ∈ P (B) be the Dirac mass. Suppose there exists some 

P ∈ P (A) such that δb = f #P . Then, 1 = δb({b}) = P (f −1({b})) = P (∅). A contradiction. Thus, such P cannot exist and P(B) is not contained in {f #P | P ∈ P (A)}.Next, we prove the second statement. For any b ∈ B, the inverse set f −1(b) is non-empty by surjectivity, and relatively closed in Dom( f ) by continuity. Since Dom(Proj) is an Fσ set, f −1(b) is also Fσ as a subset of A. By Theorem 5.3 of [92], A is σ-compact (being a countable union of compact sets), and thus f −1(b) as an Fσ subset is also σ-compact. Meanwhile, Proposition 12.4 of [92] implies that the graph of f is Borel measureable in A × B. Hence, the conditions for Theorem 35.46 of [92] are met, and it implies that the set-valued map f −1 has a Borel selection, namely a Borel measureable function g : B → A such that g(b) ∈ f −1(b) for all b ∈ B. It follows that f ◦ g is the identity function on B. For any P∗ ∈ P (B), set P = g#P∗ ∈ P (A), then f #P = P∗.

Proposition B.2. Let Ω be a finite set with |Ω| > 1, and X be a Polish space (i.e. separable and completely metrizable). There exists a Borel measureable surjection f from Ωω to X .Proof. ϕ between [0 , 1] and C. Denote by ϕω the Borel isomorphism between [0 , 1] ω and Cω , with ϕ applied entry-wise. Let o : N → N2 be any bijection, and define a homeomorphism ˜ o : Cω → C by ˜o (x(i))∞

So [92, Corollary 15.2] implies that g(X ) is a Borel subset of C, and that ˜ o ◦ ϕω is a Borel isomorphism between ψ(X ) and

# E Proof of the Separation Theorems 

## E.1 Part I 

To construct the HMM of Theorem 2.1, we need a target function that is “inherently sequential”. Let 

σ0 = (1 , 2)(3 , 4) and σ1 = (1 , 3, 5) be permutations from the alternating group A5 (using the cycle notation). Define a function F : {0, 1}∗ → { 1, . . . 5} by 

F (i1, . . . , i t) = σit ◦ · · · ◦ σi1 (1) (E.1) The group A5 is chosen since it is non-solvable, and it is a well-known result in circuit complexity theory that the wording problem of any non-solvable group is NC 1-complete [12, 145]. Specifically, let G be any non-solvable group and define the following set of sequences of group elements 

LG = (g1, . . . g n) ∈ G∗ gn ◦ · · · ◦ g1 = id 

then any NC 1 language L is strongly reducible to LG, meaning that L can be recognized by a constant-depth polynomial-size circuit family with NOT-gates, AND/OR-gates with fan-in 2, and gates that recognize LG [145]. As a straightforward corollary, computing F also requires NC 1 circuits. 97 Lemma E.1. Assume that TC 0 ⊊ NC 1. The function F cannot be computed by TC 0 circuits. Proof. Using the terminology of [145], given any subset (or language) L ⊆ { 0, 1}∗, denote by ≡L the syntactic congruence over {0, 1}∗ induced by L, namely for any x, y ∈ { 0, 1}∗, x ≡L y if and only if 

∀u, v ∈ { 0, 1}∗, uxv ∈ L ↔ uyv ∈ L

and denote by M (L) the syntactic monoid, namely the quotient {0, 1}∗/ ≡L.Define F : {0, 1}∗ → A5 by F(i1, . . . , i t) = σit ◦ · · · ◦ σi1 so that F ≡ F(·)(1). Since σ0, σ 1 generate 

A5, the subset F−1(g) is nonempty for each g ∈ A5. Consider the language LF = F −1(1). Then, one can check that x ≡LF y if and only if F(x) = F(y). Specifically, for the “only if” part, if F(x)̸ = F(y), we can choose k ∈ { 1, . . . 5} and z ∈ { 0, 1}∗ such that F(x)( k)̸ = F(y)( k) and 1 = F(z) F(x)( k) = F(zx )( k)so zx ∈ LF . Meanwhile, F(zy )( k) = F(z)( F(y)( k)) ̸ = 1 since F(z) is a bijection, so zy / ∈ L and thus 

x̸ ≡LF y. Hence, the syntactic monoid M (LF ) is isomorphic to A5. By [145, Theorem IX.1.5], LF is 

NC 1-complete. Suppose for contradiction that F can be computed by TC 0 circuits. Then, the subset F −1(1) = LF

can be recognized by some TC 0 circuit family, e.g. append an output gate to each circuit of F that outputs 1 or 0 if F (x) = 1 or ̸ = 1. It follows that all NC 1 languages, being strongly reducible to the 

TC 0 language LF , can be recognized by TC 0 circuits, a contradiction to TC 0 ⊊ NC 1.

Proof of Theorem 2.1. In terms of Definition 2.2, we define an HMM as follows. Denote by σ2 the group identity id of A5, following the notations σ0, σ 1 of (E.1). The latent vocabulary is Ω = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5} × {0, 1, 2}, with each state denoted by z = ( v, w ). The observable vocabulary is Σ = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5} ∪ {0, −1}, with the last two elements representing σ0, σ 1. Regarding the transition matrix A ∈ R15 ×7,each row A(v,w ) has three non-zero entries, ( σw(v), w ′) with w′ = 0 , 1, 2, and their probabilities are  

> 14

, 14 , 12 . The observation matrix B ∈ R15 ×7 is deterministic, such that each row B(v,w ) has only one nonzero entry. If w ∈ { 0, 1}, then this entry is −w, else this entry is v. The initial distribution λ ∈ R15 

has three non-zero entries, (1 , w ) with w = 0 , 1, 2, again with probabilities 14 , 14 , 12 .In plain terms, this HMM iteratively applies random permutations to a hidden element. At each step, it either reveals this element ( v), or applies a new permutation and reveals this permutation ( w), each case with 1 /2 probability. Suppose for contradiction that inequality (2.14) does not hold. Then, there exists some configura-tion ( L, H, d h, p ) that achieves equality. By Theorem 13 of [30], any f ∈ TF (L, H, d h, p ) is equivalent to a DLOGTIME -uniform TC 0 circuit family. Thus, there exists some constant df such that the min-imum size of a threshold circuit with depth ≤ df that simulates f over length-t inputs is polynomial in t. Note that this bound is uniform over TF (L, H, d h, p ): Denote by f = fθ the parametrization of transformers in TF (L, H, d h, p ), where θ is a O(LH 2d2

> h

)-dimensional vector of p-bit numbers. Since each entry of θ is only involved in at most t floating-point multiplications or additions for inputs of length t, the mapping ( θ, x ) 7 → fθ (x) can also be implemented by a DLOGTIME -uniform TC 0 circuit family. Denote its circuit depth upper bound by d and its circuit sizes by a polynomial s(t) of input length t. Then, all f ∈ TF (L, H, d h, p ) are uniformly bounded in circuit depth by d and in size by s(t). Denote by ¯ s(t) the minimum circuit size for any family of threshold circuits with depth upper bound d that computes the function F (E.1). Denote by sp the minimum size of a threshold circuit that computes the argmax of three floating point numbers with precision p bits (this argmax is always unique in later construction, so we do not need to consider ties). Lemma E.1 indicates that ¯ s must be superpolynomial, namely lim t→∞ ¯s(t)/t k for all k ∈ N. Thus, there exists some T such that ¯s(T ) > s (T ) + sp.By construction, P ≤TA,B,λ (x) = 4 −T for any x ∈ { 0, −1}T , and P ≤T +1

# G Unbiased Decoding Algorithm 

Supplementary to the simple but biased decoding algorithms of Section 3.3, this section describes an algorithm based on an unbiased estimation of the next-token probabilities (Proj # P )≤| x|+1 (·| x). In preparation, we first consider the ideal setting with unlimited compute and calculate each like-lihood exactly. An implementation is provided by Algorithm 1. Note that, except for the initialization, line 4 is the only place that calls the distribution P (or the model that parametrizes P ). The cache updated at line 8 works for line 5 of the next step since Proj −1 

> <ω

The resulting X(ω) is a sample of (Proj # P )( ·| [x]). Alternatively, one can terminate whenever the decoded token Xt = ⟨eos ⟩, some “end of sentence” symbol, and return X(t).103 Algorithm 1 Decoding algorithm, ideal setting

Sampling-based decoding replaces lines 4 and 6 of Algorithm 1 with Monte-Carlo estimators. Re-placing line 6 is necessary, since Proj −1 

> <ω

(x) must be intractably large as aforementioned. However, the cost of line 4 depends on the size of Φ( z, a ) and thus on the choice of Proj, e.g. its size is constant for Example 2.2, while unbounded for Example 2.3. For simplicity, we replace line 6 anyway. Ideally, this algorithm should proceed incrementally to save compute, similar to the decoding algorithms of Section 3.3. Specifically, when estimating line 6 by sampling, with some sampler Q, the algorithm preferably should obtain each sample Z(t,i ) by updating the earlier sample Z(t−1,i ), instead of sampling Q(·| X(t)) from scratch for each t. So, an ideal algorithm is to first sample {Z(0 ,i )}ni=1 from 

Q(·| X(0) ) with X(0) = x, and then incrementally extend Z(0 ,i ) to Z(t,i ) and X(0) to X(t). These Z(t,i )

should remain i.i.d. samples of Q(·| X(t)) for all t to enable Monte-Carlo estimation. Technically, such incremental procedure requires a conditional distribution B(·| z) such that, given a sample Z ∼ Q(·| X(t−1) ), the distribution of ZS with S ∼ B(·| Z) is exactly Q(·| X(t)). However, the following proposition indicates that this constraint makes the sampler Q suboptimal. 

Proposition G.1. Given the setting of Theorem 2.2 and any sampler Q ∈ Q Proj , if for any x ∈ Σ∗

and a ∈ Σ such that xa ∈ Dom( Q), there exists a conditional distribution B : Proj −1 

> <ω

(x) → P (Ω ∗)

such that 

Q(·| xa ) = law( ZS ), S ∼ B(·| Z), Z ∼ Q(·| x)

then Q is a causal sampler and in general must differ from the posterior sampler Q∗.Proof. The existence of B implies that 

Q(·| x) = Π Proj −1 

> <ω (x)

#Q(·| xa )Applying this equality recursively over the next token a, we obtain the identity (3.32) for any x, x ′ ∈

Dom( Q) such that x ⊑ x′. Thus, Q is a causal sampler. By Theorem 3.1, Q∗ is in general not a causal sampler. Hence, in order to be able to approximate the variance-minimizing sampler Q∗, we sample Q(·| X(t))from scratch for each t. The resulting sampling-based decoding algorithm is Algorithm 2. # H KL Divergence from Finite to Infinite Sequences 

This section provides a supplementary result to Section 3.4, that the cross entropy loss (3.15) can be interpreted in terms of the KL divergence with the latent distribution P , not just with Proj # P .We assume that P∗ is supported on a prefix-free subset. This is typical in practice, for instance the samples for pretraining are usually arranged to have the same length, and the samples for finetuning are appended with an ⟨eos ⟩ token that only appears at the end, so the sample sets in both cases are prefix-free.

# I Properties of Gradient Estimation 

This section provides the derivation of the optimal samplers and expected error of the gradient esti-mator in Section 3.4. To avoid degeneracy, we make the following assumption. It can be satisfied, for instance, if we implement Pf and the samplers by Transformer LLMs. 

Assumption 2. The latent distribution Pf satisfies the following: for any x ∈ Dom( Q∗) and z ∈

> <ω

(x) is finite, this is equivalent to sprt Q∗(·| x) ⊆ sprt ˜Q′).

Lemma J.1. Let A, B be two sets such that |A|, |B| > 1, and let R be a relation between A, B (namely a subset of A × B). If each a ∈ A is contained in at least one pair r ∈ R, and each b ∈ B is contained in at least one r ∈ R, then there exist two pairs, (a, b ), (a′, b ′) ∈ R, such that a̸ = a′ and b̸ = b′.Proof. Let a, a ′ be two distinct elements of A, and let ( a, b ), (a′, b ′) be two pairs in R. If b̸ = b′, then we are done. Else, since |B| > 1, there exist some b′′ ∈ B such that b′′ ̸ = b. Choose a pair ( a′′ , b ′′ ) ∈ R.If a′′ ̸ = a, then we can go with ( a, b ), (a′′ , b ′′ ). Else, we can go with ( a, b ′′ ), (a′, b ).

# K From Global Objective to Instantaneous Loss 

In Section 5.6.3, we approximate the optimization of the total cost (5.26) with gradient descent on the running cost (5.27). This appendix provides an informal discussion of when this approximation may hold. To simplify the analysis, we use continuous time and express problem (5.26) abstractly by the value function 

V (x0, t 0) = min 

> p∈P(x0,t 0)

Z ∞

> t0

f (p(t)) dt (K.1) 

P(x0, t 0) = p : [ t0, ∞) → Rd p(t0) = x0, ∥p∥Lip ≤ 1

Specifically, the train steps s are converted to continuous time t (as an abuse of notation, this is not the elapsed time in Section 5.6.3), the parameter θ becomes a point x ∈ Rd, the update trajectory (θs)∞ 

> s=0

becomes a path p : [ t0, ∞) → Rd, the admissible set of trajectories Θ becomes the set of paths 

P (with prescribed initialization), and the running cost C(θs)Ts(θs) is generalized to a nonnegative smooth function f . The Lipschitz bound ∥p∥Lip ≤ 1 represents the constraint on Θ; since typically one avoids having excessively large parameter updates when training LLMs (e.g. with gradient clipping and normalization), it seems acceptable to model the update trajectories as 1-Lipschitz paths. To ensure that the total cost (5.26) is finite, the running cost must converge to zero. So we assume for convenience that the set of minimizers S = {x | f (x) = 0 } is nonempty. It follows that the value function V is finite and nonnegative, and V = 0 if and only if on S. Also, the definition of V implies 114 that it does not depend on t, so ∂tV ≡ 0 and we write ∇V = ∇xV . By Hamilton-Jacobi-Bellman equation, 0 = −∂tV = min  

> ∥v∥≤ 1

∇xV · v + f = −∥∇ V ∥ + f

So the value function satisfies the eikonal equation |∇ V | = f .The minimizing path of (K.1) is driven by ˙ pt = − ∇V (pt)  

> ∥∇ V(pt)∥

. Meanwhile, gradient descent on the running cost can be modeled by the path ˙pt = −∇ f (pt). We say that two paths are equal if their images overlap, so the problem becomes under what condition there exists a non-negative function λ

such that ∇V = λ∇f .Suppose the gradient norm of f depends only on the value of f , namely there exist some differ-entiable function ψ such that ∥∇ f ∥ = ψ(f ). Then, the velocity field f  

> ∥∇ f∥

∇f is a gradient field: In terms of differential forms 

d

 f

∥∇ f ∥ df 



= 0 + dψ (f )−1 ∧ f df = 0 So there exists some function ˜V such that ∇ ˜V = f  

> ∥∇ f∥

∇f , and in particular, |∇ ˜V | = f . By uniqueness of the solution to |∇ V | = f , the functions V, ˜V are equal up to additive constant. Thus, ∇V = λ∇f

with λ = f / ∥f ∥.Hence, it is possible for the gradient descent path to coincide with the optimal path for the total cost. Despite that the condition ∥∇ f ∥ = ψ(f ) probably does not hold in practice, this argument indicates that our approximation is at least not entirely unfounded. 

# L Implementation of the Preliminary Experiment 

This appendix lists the implementation details of Section 7. For all components of all three models, the maximum learning rate is 10 −5, weight decay is 0 .1 to align with [176], gradient clipping is 1 .0, batch size is 1920, and latent sampling size (not applicable to SFT) is n = 8. All models are trained for 1000 steps, and each evaluation takes 10 steps. The training samples and test samples are disjoint. During training, the SFT model is evaluated every 5 steps, and the two slow thinking models are evaluated every 20 steps, so the curves in Figure 7.1 have different resolutions. The learning rate schedule is the commonly used cosine schedule, such that learning rate increases linearly from 0 to the maximum value in the first 5% steps, and then decay to 10% of the maximum value. The optimizer is AdamW [108] with the default hyparameters β = (0 .9, 0.999). To save GPU memory, we use the gradient parallelism and optimizer parallelism of DeepSpeed ZeRO-2 [131]. Figure L.1 shows the input formats of all trained models. Denote these four formats by xx r ,

x⟨s⟩y⟨/s ⟩xr , x⟨s⟩y and ⟨r⟩xr ⟨/r ⟩x⟨s⟩y, with the delimiters denoted by ⟨s⟩, ⟨/s ⟩, ⟨r⟩, ⟨/r ⟩.One issue is that in general, adapting an LLM to a new format could cause a large increase in the losses at the beginning of training, which could last for a few thousand steps. Thus, a smooth initialization is needed, and we adapt the gating trick of [186] to our setting. Empirically, this trick largely alleviates the initial loss increase of training slow thinking models, as their loss curves are close to the SFT curve in Figure 7.1. To prepare for the construction, let f be the Transformer of the latent model or sampler, and let d be its feature dimension. Given any input sequence z, denote the |z| × d

feature matrix prior to the unembedding layer of f by fpre (z), and the |z| × | Ω| logit matrix after the unembedding layer by fpost (z). Recall that the standard routine of SFT is to compute the following 

|xr | × | Σ| matrix to compute the loss 

fpost (xx r )[−| xr |− 1,−1] 

where [ −| xr | − 1, −1] means taking the rows |x| − 1, . . . |x| + |xr | − 1 of the matrix. The −1 means that we are using the feature vector at the previous token to predict each token. The gating trick is implemented as follows: If f comes from the latent model Pθ , we modify its output to 

pfast ⊙ fpost (xx r )[−| xr |− 1,−1] + pslow ⊙ fpost 

 x⟨s⟩y⟨/s ⟩xr

  

> [−| xr|− 1,−1]

(L.1) and the gatings pfast , p slow are |xr | × 1 matrices computed by 

 pfast 

pslow 



= softmax  MLP  fpre (xx r )[−| xr |− 1,−1] ⊕ fpre (x⟨s⟩y⟨/s ⟩xr )[−| xr |− 1,−1] 

 (L.2) 115 (Query x)(Response xr ) (Query x)Start thinking: (Thought y)End of thinking (Response xr )    

> (Query x)Start thinking: (Decode a thought)
> Start of reference: (Response xr)End of reference (Query x)Start thinking: (Decode a thought)
> 1

Figure L.1: The input formats of the SFT model, the latent models, the predictive sampler, and the explanatory sampler, respectively. where ⊕ means concatenating the matrices in the feature dimension. The MLP is a 3-layer multilayer perceptron with SiLU activation, and with input dimension 2 d, hidden dimension 2 d, and output dimension 2. The softmax is applied to the 2 output entries for each row, so that pfast + pslow ≡ 1. The MLP accounts for only 1 .33% of the total parameters in f , so its influence on the capacity of the slow thinking models should be negligible and thus is acceptable here. It is trained from scratch, and its output layer is a PyTorch linear layer with weight initialized to all 0 and bias to [2 , −2]. Thus, at the beginning of training, pslow = sigmoid( −4) < 0.02, and the model mostly uses fast thinking. Similarly, if f comes from the explanatory sampler, then we modify its output to 

pfast ⊙ fpost (x⟨s⟩y)[−| y|− 1,−1] + pslow ⊙ fpost 

 ⟨r⟩xr ⟨/r ⟩x⟨s⟩y

> [−| y|− 1,−1]

 pfast 

pslow 



= softmax  MLP  fpre (x⟨s⟩y)[−| y|− 1,−1] ⊕ fpre (⟨r⟩xr ⟨/r ⟩x⟨s⟩y)[−| y|− 1,−1] 

 

The latent model and the explanatory sampler use separate MLPs. The initialization is the same as above, so the explanatory sampler initially behaves like a predictive sampler. This gating is also applied to the decoding stage (7.2) of the explanatory sampler (after all, at the implementation level, the decoding of Transformer is repeated encoding). One may worry about the doubled computation cost due to the gating trick. Nevertheless, this trick is only used for finetuning. If a slow thinking model with explanatory sampler(s) is trained from scratch, then the initial loss increase due to new formats would not happen. Even for finetuned models, one may reduce the inference cost by distilling a finetuned model with gating to a base model without gating. Another technical detail is that gradient clipping treats the entire slow thinking model (the latent model and the sampler) as one model. Specifically, while the gradient of the SFT model is clipped to 

vSFT / max(1 , ∥vSFT ∥), the gradient of each slow thinking is clipped to 

vlatent ⊕ vsampler 

max  1, ∥vlatent ⊕ vsampler ∥

instead of vlatent 

max  1, ∥vlatent ∥ ⊕ vsampler 

max  1, ∥vsampler ∥

In practice, one can simply wrap the parameters of the latent model and sampler into one optimizer, instead of two. The consideration is that separate clipping makes the training worse, in particular for the explanatory sampler, as depicted in Figure L.2 (Left). The mechanism of this failure has not been determined yet; perhaps the model components need a smaller learning rate. 116 Figure L.2: Left: Comparison of combined clipping and separate clipping. The sampling size for this experiment is n = 16. Right: Comparison of training with sampling size n = 8 and n = 16. Next, an experiment is performed to study the influence of the sampling size n. As plotted in Figure L.2 (Right), increasing n from 8 to 16 improves the performance of both slow thinking models, but the loss gains tend to become saturated and the relative improvement of the explanatory sampler becomes smaller: Lfast − Lexpl 

Lfast − Lpred 

− 1 = 1.7345 − 1.7278 1.7345 − 1.7301 − 1 ≈ 52% Such saturation is expected, since the samplers improve the training progress only in an indirect manner: More expressive samplers such as the explanatory samplers can further reduce the divergences 

χ2(Q∗(·| x)∥Q(·| x)) and thus the estimation error of the training gradient (3.24) (also cf. Figure 4.1), and then the latent model Pθ with more accurate gradients may converge faster. No matter what sampler we use (as long as sprt Q∗(·| x) ⊆ sprt Q(·| x)), the gradient estimation error always converges to its minimum as n → ∞ , so the advantage of more expressive samplers diminishes. 

Figure L.3: Test loss vs. TFLOPs. The solid curves are the same as those in Figure 7.1 (Left), except that their horizontal axes change from “training step” to “total TFLOPs consumed at the current step”. Similarly, the axes of the dotted curves are “the ideal TFLOPs without gating”. Finally, we consider the compute cost of these experiments, measured by TFLOPs (10 12 floating-point operations). It is not the most realistic measure, since the time cost consists of not only the compute time, but also the data transfer time. The latter becomes dominant during LLM decoding (e.g. the generation of chain-of-thoughts for slow thinking models) and is known as the memory wall 117 [64, 171]. Nevertheless, since decoding (or more generally, sequential computation) seems to be an indispensable feature from the perspective of the representation hierarchy, it is reasonable to expect that technological advances will be made to alleviate the data transfer time, e.g. through in-memory and near-memory computation [170, 189]. Thus, we adopt TFLOPs, and Figure L.3 plots the test loss curves of Figure 7.1 (Left) with respect to the TFLOPs during training. The TFLOPs are calculated as follows. The number of floating-point operations of one forward pass of a Transformer LLM with gated MLP layers can be estimated by 

NLLM (lin , l out ) = 2 · L ·  lin · (2 d2 + 2 dd hHkv + 3 dW ) + 2 · l2in 

2 d + lout · nvocab d

where lin is the input length and lout is the amount of tokens kept for logit computation. The derivation of NLLM can be found in the appendices of [179, 117]. For Qwen2.5-7B base model [176], the constants are L = 28 layers, H = 28 heads, Hkv = 4 key-value heads, hidden dimension d = 3584, head dimension 

dh = 128, MLP width W = 18944, vocabulary size nvocab = 152064. For SFT training, the compute cost would be 3 · NLLM (lq + lr , l r ), where lq , l r are the lengths of the query and response, and the coefficient 3 = 1 + 2 means that the backward pass incurs twice the cost of the forward pass [117]. For decoding, the compute cost would be NLLM (lq + lr , l r ). The extra cost due to the gating mechanism can be estimated by 

Ngate (lout ) = 2 · lout · (LMLP − 1)(2 d)2 + 3 · lout · nvocab 

The first term is the cost of the forward pass of the MLP (L.2), with hidden dimension 2 d and depth 

LMLP = 3. The −1 is due to the last linear layer, which can be omitted since its output dimension is just 2. The second term is the cost of the weighted averaging (L.1). The following constants 

lchunk = lthought = 128 , l⟨s⟩ = l⟨r⟩ = 4 , l⟨/s ⟩ = l⟨/r ⟩ = 5 are the lengths of the text chunks (simulated queries and responses), thoughts, and aforementioned delimiters (with respect to the tokenizer of Qwen2.5-7B). For SFT, the compute cost of each training sample is approximately 

NSFT = 3 · CLM (2 lchunk , l chunk ) ≈ 10 .48 TFLOPs For the slow thinking model with predictive sampler, the per-sample cost is approximately 

Npred = (1 + 3) · NLLM (lchunk + l⟨s⟩ + lthought , l thought )+ 3 ·  NLLM (2 lchunk + l⟨s⟩ + lthought + l⟨/s ⟩, l chunk ) + NLLM (2 lchunk , l chunk ) + Ngate (lchunk )

≈ 40 .64 TFLOPs where 1 + 3 is for decoding and training. For the slow thinking model with explanatory sampler, the per-sample cost is approximately 

Nexpl = (1 + 3) ·  NLLM (lchunk + l⟨s⟩ + lthought , l thought )+ NLLM (2 lchunk + l⟨r⟩ + l⟨/r ⟩ + l⟨s⟩ + lthought , l thought )

+ 3 ·  NLLM (2 lchunk + l⟨s⟩ + lthought + l⟨/s ⟩, l chunk ) + NLLM (2 lchunk , l chunk ) + Ngate (lchunk )

≈ 62 .16 TFLOPs For reference, we also consider the ideal setting without the issue of loss increase at the beginning (e.g. when training from scratch or given a better way to initialize finetuning) and thus without the need of gating. The per-sample costs of the slow thinking models would become 

Npred-ideal = (1 + 3) · NLLM (lchunk + l⟨s⟩ + lthought , l thought )+ 3 · NLLM (2 lchunk + l⟨s⟩ + lthought + l⟨/s ⟩, l chunk )

≈ 30 .08 TFLOPs 

Nexpl-ideal = (1 + 3) · NLLM (2 lchunk + l⟨r⟩ + l⟨/r ⟩ + l⟨s⟩ + lthought , l thought )+ 3 · NLLM (2 lchunk + l⟨s⟩ + lthought + l⟨/s ⟩, l chunk )

≈ 37 .31 TFLOPs The cumulative cost at each training step s would be the per-sample cost times the batch size 1920 times s. Thus, we obtain the five curves in Figure L.3. 118
